# SOLIDWORKS 2024 Essential Training

> SOLIDWORKS is the premier product-design software—enabling you to deliver innovative solutions to market faster. In this course, Gabriel Corbett teaches you how to build ready-to-manufacture parts and assemblies as well as detailed drawings and itemized bills of materials in SOLIDWORKS 2024. Learn how to create 2D sketches and then extrude, revolve, loft, and sweep these features into 3D objects. 

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training) | 10h | 5K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

### Introduction

#### [Learn 3D CAD modeling with SOLIDWORKS 2024](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/learn-3d-cad-modeling-with-solidworks-2024?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/learn-3d-cad-modeling-with-solidworks-2024?u=76281980&t=0)** - Hi, I'm Gabe Corbett, and welcome to [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) 2024 Essential Training. This course is for anyone looking to get into the world of 3D design, and will benefit new users, as well as those transitioning from other software packages. Are you ready to upgrade your skills and learn SOLIDWORKS 2024? Join me for this [LinkedIn](../../Skills/Web%20Development/LinkedIn.md) Learning course. You'll get up to speed quickly on creating complex parts, assemblies, and drawings. We'll cover all the key tools and techniques to create two dimensional sketches that'll become the foundation for 3D objects. Next, we'll look at modeling 3D features by extruding or revolving sketches into 3D parts, followed by creating more complex objects using the sweep, loft and surface tools. And that's just the beginning. Join me on LinkedIn Learning for SOLIDWORKS 2024 Essential Training.

SolidWorks is both a history and feature-based parametric modeling program. This software uses a simple set of tools and commands to help you design parts, assemblies, and drawings. Now on your screen here, you should see something that looks a lot like this icon here. It's called SolidWorks. And go ahead and just double click on that, and let SolidWorks open up. So if you're making just a block like this, you can get crazy with that. But if you have a assembly that has hundreds of thousands components, you might want to turn this off 'cause it's going to slow SolidWorks down. Okay. Most the time I turn all these things off while I'm working, unless I want to do like a presentation, or take some screen capture of my actual part and put it in a presentation, they all just slow things down. >
> **[9:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/launching-solidworks-for-the-first-time?u=76281980&t=560)** I want to point out while we're here. The first one is, if you click on it, it just kind of flies out. It's a fly out bar. So here's the welcome to SolidWorks. These are some of the resources you have. Directly below that is going to be your design library. This is where you can store information, things like parts or annotations, things like that. They're available here. And if you're like working on a drawing, you can drag these notes into your drawings. If you're working with assemblies, you can bring in like a whole mold base. You know, you can just click on this thing and bring in an entire mold base. You can also have features, forming tools, parts, [Routing](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Routing.md), whatever you want. They can all be over here in your design library. You can use the default library, or you can create your own and add your own components. Directly below that's going to be the toolbox. And SolidWorks has got a preloaded library of toolbox components, which includes fasteners and screws and nuts and washers and bearings, and everything else you can think of. And you can just drag and drop 'em into your assembly. Below that is 3D content central. So a lot of manufacturers make 3D CAD files available to you for free. So instead of designing them all yourself, especially if you're working with an off the shelf component, download it, right, and put into your assembly, it's going to save you a lot of time. Same thing with the 3D components. PartSupply is another supplier of components that you can just drag and drop into your models. Directly below that is just another way to get to your file explorer so you can see what's open in SolidWorks and so on. They'll show up over here. Below that is appearances, right? So I can apply materials and backgrounds to my part. Right over here I can say, "Hey, I want to make this thing plastic. I want a medium glass plastic." And you can just drag and drop that over into your part and say, "Hey, I want to apply that to the entire part."
>
> **[10:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/launching-solidworks-for-the-first-time?u=76281980&t=652)** And now that appearance has been added to that part. Okay? Keep in mind there's also material over on this side here. And if you apply a material here, not only does it give you the material properties added to this, but it also grabs the appearance. So two things you can do there. And then the last thing over here is going to be custom properties, which we'll be jumping into later. So anyways, hopefully that gives you a good overview of exactly what tools are available, at least the locations of them. I know you're obviously have a lot of questions and we're going to be diving into all these little sections as we go through the course, and you'll be learning and using these tools as we go through the course. But at least now you know basically where all these things are.

#### [Saving, renaming, and managing files](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/saving-renaming-and-managing-files?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/saving-renaming-and-managing-files?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For this movie, let's go ahead and learn about how [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) links things together. Now, SolidWorks is a a hundred percent name based linked software. So what that means is you have something like an assembly right over here at the top level. And then below that is going to have individual parts. You can have multiple parts, there's many parts as you want. You can also have other sub assemblies that then have parts of their own right. And each one of these things can have its own drawing. So here's a drawing of that assembly and you can have a drawing of a part as well. Or of course you can have a drawing of a subassembly. So any of these things can have their own drawings. They all have their own properties and they all have their own names. Now, SolidWorks is very concerned about the name. So if you name this one part one, this one, part two, this one, part three, the assembly is going to be looking for those individual names, right? If you were to go and change the name of that file on SolidWorks in like [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) Explorer for instance, to something else and SolidWorks didn't know about that change, well it's going to be looking for that part number and it can't find it, right? It's going to be looking always for that name and it's looking exactly where it was saved last, wherever that path is and whatever that file name is, is the path it's looking for. So you definitely don't want to jump into like Windows Explorer or any other system and change names of assemblies. I always recommend doing something like this. Draw out your assembly first, figure out what your top level assembly is going to be, what number or name you're going to give that one. Figure out what the basic sub assemblies might be from there, what parts will be involved in there, name them, what they're going to be, give them a part number ahead of time. Figure all that out ahead of time.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/saving-renaming-and-managing-files?u=76281980&t=92)** It will save you so much time in the long run. #### [Measuring](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/measuring?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/measuring?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you're working on a new part in [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md), more than likely, you're going to want to do some measuring. You're going to want to figure out how big a feature is, how long something is, and especially if you're working on a part that you haven't created, maybe somebody else created the part and you're just trying to get familiar with that part. And if your tabs aren't in the same order, that's fine because SolidWorks is configurable. So if you just choose center-to-center, I can choose something like this, center of that circle or that center of that cylinder, and then it's going to show you the exactly the center distance of 1.73, but it's also going to break this down into the X and a Y components, or the actual Z and Y components, in this case here, of what those are. How do you get from one thing to the other thing? >
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/measuring?u=76281980&t=93)** hey, I want choose like the length of this to, or sorry, the length from there to there, you can get a minimum distance here. I'm actually choosing this bottom face. So you might want to choose a different selection in this case, maybe something like the edge down here and the face or the edge up here. And now you've got something going right down the center of that, giving you the actual distance. So it really depends upon what you choose. If you choose faces, if you choose edges, if you choose points, the tool is going to adjust to whatever you happen to be working on. Also, you can use this tool if you happen to be creating something else. So over here, if I said, hey, I'm going to start a new sketch right over here on this face, I'm going to start a circle. #### [Interface improvements for SOLIDWORKS 2024](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/interface-improvements-for-solidworks-2024?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/interface-improvements-for-solidworks-2024?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Each year, [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) gets just a little bit better. And in this year, there's been some great improvements. In fact, one of the things that people have been asking for for literally years and years and years and years that SolidWorks has never delivered on, they have finally come out with. So I've been to a bunch of the SolidWorks Worlds, these are the World Conferences where everybody gets together and they, you know, they ask for what's the best things and what the most requested improvements of SolidWorks are. And one of those things is backwards compatibility. For years you have not been able to actually save a document into a previous version. And they did come up with that a few years ago and it didn't really work that good, but now we actually have the option to save back for two versions of the software. So we happen to be working in SolidWorks 2024, so I can save as a 2024 document, a 2023, or 2022 document from SolidWorks now. So let me show you how that works. Right over here under file, right, we can go to Save As, and normally you can just save as a regular SolidWorks part. But if you click on the little dropdown here, I can go over here and save it as SolidWorks 2022 or SolidWorks 2023. So that's the first time this has ever happened, and it actually works pretty good. Now, there are a few things inside of SolidWorks that they've added, some new features, that if you save it back a couple versions, those features may not work. And so if you do that, it's going to give you a little warning that says, "Hey, some of these things might get deleted or might not work if you switch back to an earlier version of the software." But at least we can do it, because I've seen a lot of problems in design processes when people will upgrade their software and then not everybody's upgrading it. Somebody opens the file and then all of a sudden (laughs) nobody else can open the file
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/interface-improvements-for-solidworks-2024?u=76281980&t=92)** because it's a future version. There's a lot of things that this is going to solve. But there's a lot of other improvements inside of SolidWorks. And I want to point out, we don't have time to go through all of them. It's a massive document of all the cool things that they've added. But if you're interested, you can go up here and click on Help. There's a bunch of different things here, but you can click on the What's New. There's also the Introducing SolidWorks PDF as well. But I'm going to go ahead and choose the What's New PDF and let that thing open up right over here. And I don't care what's new with [Microsoft](../../Instructors/Artificial%20Intelligence%20(AI)/Microsoft.md) Edge, let's turn that off. But right over here I have the top enhancements, and I can just kind of [Zoom](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Zoom.md) through this document here. You can see that it's got a lot, a lot of pages, in fact, 171 pages in this thing. And you can see all the things that are going on, all the improvements inside of SolidWorks. And you basically get this every single year. So every new year, every new version of the software or something great comes out, and you can look through here and take a look at it. But definitely take a look at this document. If you're looking at the improvements and see what the most important things are to you and if they apply to your organization or what you're doing, definitely take a look and learn about them. Again, I don't have time to go into all those improvements 'cause there's just so many each year, but that backwards compatibility, that's an awesome one and I'm happy to see that one finally in SolidWorks 2024.

And then a dimension from here to here is the size of the block, right? So some basic dimensions like that is all we really need to add so that we know exactly how big the block is. Now how about some notes, right? We need to know what the material is. And normally you're going to add that down here, like in the material block or the finish, who's drawing it, what the part number is, what the revision number, you got to fill out this entire block. You also should come up with a template, right? With your company logo and your name, stuff like that.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/making-your-first-cad-drawing?u=76281980&t=186)** So all this needs to be filled out to make a drawing. And then you might want to type in something like material and then define what the material is, right? You might want to do the finish, whatever it is. You're going to add those notes up here on the screen. We also have the design library over here. So if you have a library and you have some annotations, you can add or pull notes out. So I have like a note right over here. It says, this is a note for material. So you could say, Hey, this material is, it's aluminum and this finish is going to be a black anodized finish and so on. >
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/making-your-first-cad-drawing?u=76281980&t=280)** similar to like [Metadata](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Metadata.md) that you're going to have something like the title, the drawing number, the revision. So the custom properties are what's tied to the parts. So I can do something like a description. How about a part number and how about a revision, right? So there's a couple things I can do there. That's right under here, under custom properties, properties, click okay. And now if I go back to the drawing, notice that information automatically shows up here on my drawing. So now I've got the title, I've got the drawing number or revision. And if you want to continue to fill things out, you can add the name of the person who drew it and the date and checked by and all this other stuff over here. But once you've got like a nice template that has your company name, all that, everything, then you're going to continue to use that over and over again, right? But the part number, the part name, the revision, that's always going to change per the part you're making, right? So keep that in mind. Okay? So anyways, that's the basic steps for creating a drawing. So you can create a drawing from any type of part
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/making-your-first-cad-drawing?u=76281980&t=373)** or any type of an assembly. You want to make sure you're adding notes, you want to make sure you're adding dimensions and making sure you've got the right tolerances and things like that. And that's pretty much all there is to creating a great drawing inside of SOLIDWORKS.

>

It's going to take a circle, it's going to revolve around that line and it's going to take this rectangle and revolve around the line. So just like the extrude command, the sketch is separate from the feature, right? The sketch itself is just defining what the shapes are that you're going to be working with, right? And then the feature itself is how far you're going
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-revolved-features?u=76281980&t=188)** to revolve that thing around. #### [Finding mass properties](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/finding-mass-properties?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/finding-mass-properties?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] What does something weigh? What's the mass of something that you're working on? It's a hard question because it really depended upon what the shape of this thing is, as well as what is it made out of. If you have something very complicated, like say it's like a human body and you say, "How much does something weigh in the human body?" Well, it's very complicated to figure that out because we're so many, we're made up of so many different composites of materials, right? But if you have something very straightforward and simple like this, it's a block of iron or cast iron or steel or something like that, it's a lot easier to figure that out. But if it's a weird shape, like this one here, there's no real easy math that's going to allow us to just calculate this. Where if it's like a brick, and you know the weight or the mass of the brick or the density of the brick, it's easy to figure it out. Just length times width, times height to figure out the volume times, whatever the mass of the brick is, at least the density of the brick to get that information. But something like this is way, way, way harder. Well, [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) has lots of great tools behind the scenes that can figure this all out for us, by clicking on mass properties, right over here, mass properties, it's going to say, "Hey, this thing weighs 10 pounds, or 9.99 pounds." But you might be saying, "Well, where is it getting this density from? Where's that density of pounds per cubic inch?" Well, guess what? It's, we haven't even applied a material here, so, it must be using some kind of default material, right? So if I change the material from that to maybe, how about like cast alloy steel or something like that? That looks okay. So now we have a cast alloy steel kettlebell. All right, now if I come up here to the mass properties of that, and now all of a sudden it weighs 72.92 pounds, so got a lot heavier, right?
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/finding-mass-properties?u=76281980&t=94)** So it's a pretty heavy kettlebell. And now if I wanted to refine this a little bit better, if I wanted to, maybe I'm trying to make a 65 pound kettlebell, right? Where I have some target that I'm working towards, or maybe I try to make something that's not over 70 pounds, I'm trying to hit like right where that is. So you can adjust this shape by adding and removing material from the shape. You can go back in here, look at, maybe look at your revolve and see, "Hey, can I make this slightly smaller or slightly bigger to hit a weight target?" Right? But once you've established what the material is, now we have to just change the volume of that shape. One other thing I want to point out while we're here is there's a great way to keep track of that, and that's called a sensor. So right over here is sensors. If you right click on it, you say add sensor, and you can figure out what that actually is. So here's mass properties you can do, you can do [Simulation](../../Skills/Hardware/Simulation.md) data, dimension, you can be kind of watching any of these things. So mass properties, sure, here's the current value of 72.92. And you can say, "Hey, I'd like to make an alert," right? So gimme an alert, if this is greater than 70 pounds, right? I'm trying to hit a 70 pound kettlebell. Click OK. And hey, go ding, ding, ding. Hey, your alert, you've got an alert here that's saying, "Hey, this thing is above," it's overweight, right? And so we need to remove some weight or some material from this. You might want to do that right here at the bottom. I can choose something like this, start a sketch, and I can say, "Hey, I'm going to remove a little cylindrical section here." And figuring out the exact weight of a cylinder is going to be a lot easier, right? But let's just go ahead and just try to just create an extrude cut and let's just come in here and just do a blind
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/finding-mass-properties?u=76281980&t=188)** and let's just going to come in and maybe like a quarter in. Let's try that far. So 0.4. Click OK, and now look at the weight. So I got to, notice it didn't change. I have to rebuild it. And of course it's still giving me, "Hey, you got a little bit lighter, but it's still overweight," right? So that's where you might want to come in here and just iterate a few times. I'm going to keep making this thing, you know, maybe a little bit bigger. Right? Click OK, rebuild, and making sure where we're at, right? So we're still above that weight, right? So now we maybe want to come in here and adjust a little further. So now it's going to go in a little deeper, click OK. And now it doesn't complain, right? It says, "Hey, okay, you're okay. Now you're actually too light," right? So you have to keep playing with that and removing and adjusting the weight of this thing or changing the diameter, something like that. But you're able to keep track of what that is using that sensor. And you can of course go back and keep adding and weighing things, but we really can use the weight and mass of something. And of course you can go back up here as well and evaluate it with the mass properties and get all that detail and get into the moments of inertia and all those other things down here that are available. So SolidWorks really does provide us a lot of really great tools for not only analyzing the mass, the size, the weight of something, and whatever those moments of inertia are, we can get just a ton of data from SolidWorks and hopefully they'll help you when you're working through your designs.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-spline-tool?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Splines are some of the most powerful, but also some of the most complicated sketch entities that you can create and work with. So let me show you how they work. So right over here, I've got a sketch going here and I've got the spline tool right up here. And there's a couple different options for splines, right? I have the regular spline, I also have these style spline, as well as an equation driven curve. We'll be covering that in the future video, but right now, let's focus on the regular spline. So with the regular spline, you kind of click where you want to start it and notice it starts that as a straight line, and click again, and now you kind of bend through there, click again in a couple places and then hit Escape when you're done. So you have these kind of control points at the end and you can move these points around, and notice as soon as you click on them, you get this little, it's like a direction vector and you can actually pull on that direction vector, you can rotate it and you can have some effect. So the longer the line is, the more control that direction vector has over that shape. And, of course, you can grab this thing over here and you can start twisting and doing things like that. So the secret, at least that I've learned from working with splines, is the least amount of control points the better, right? So if you can get away with only having the two ends, it's probably the best. So let me show you how that works. So if you just create a line like this one here, hit Escape, now you have a straight line. So you're thinking, well, that's not really a spline. Well, it really is because each side of the spline has a control point. So notice over here I can grab that control point and I can start moving that spline around a little bit and I can get a really nice curve, right?
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-spline-tool?u=76281980&t=93)** And you're just determining that curve by these two handles or these two controls. And so I really like to use the spline in this manner here, just having a straight line at first and then controlling it 'cause especially if you have like a lot of control points, you can really get a spline way out of control really quickly. And let me show you a few other things to make it even more out of control. If you come up here to tools, first actually let's select a spline like that guy right there. Come over here to tools, come down here to spline tools and you can see how many things you can actually do with this. So you can add tangency control, curvature control, you can add points in there, you can simplify it, you can convert from one to the other. You could fit a spline to another shape. So you have all the stuff you can do, right? There's a lot of modifications and handles that you can add and change. And you know, splines are really good when you're working with surfaces and you're trying to make some free flowing curves and you can really get some complicated shapes using splines. But I do want to caution you if you're using a spline, there's no real good way to dimension it or to define the spline. So if you're trying to define a surface, you're not going to be able to put a radius on that, for instance, it's going to have to have a lot more complicated file or documentation when you're actually using a spline. So if you have the option and you can use just regular curves that have radiuses, it'll be a lot better off. And in fact, you can do a compound curve with a bunch of regular radiuses that a lot of times will replicate a very similar thing to a spline, which will be a lot easier to manufacture down the road. But if you're using splines,
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-spline-tool?u=76281980&t=185)** if you're doing some complex curves or maybe some plastic parts, something like that that are molded, you can really get some really great and useful different sketches that you can use. Now, before I move on here, I do want to show that there's some other types of splines. So the style spline is another version of this one. So I'm going to [Zoom](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Zoom.md) out a little bit and you click over here and this one is actually going to be a control polygon. I feel like they're a little bit easier to work with and you can get very nice curves going forward.

#### [Equation driven curve](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/equation-driven-curve?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/equation-driven-curve?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For this video, we're going to look at the equation-driven curve. So what exactly is an equation-driven curve? Well that's something that we're going to find out. So right over here, I'm in the sketch tool. I'm going to go ahead and create a front plane. And right over here under where the spline is, is I'm going to come down here to equation-driven curve. So there it is. All right, so what does this mean? So basically your curve's going to be a combination between X and Y. So if you say X is equal to Y, it's going to be a straight line. So here's my equation. I have two different ways to do it. I can say an explicit or a parametric. So my first one's going to be just something based on X. So I'm going to make sure that's a little X. And then I'm going to go ahead and say where I want to start from. So I'm going to say zero. And let's say I'm going to go to five. So that's going to define my curve. And notice right over here on the screen, I have this straight line because X is equal to five. Well what if you want to make your equation a little bit more fancy? How about X squared? So you say X to power of two, and now all of a sudden you've got this like parabola shape. What if you want to do something even more fancy? How about we do sine? So sine of X. And now all of a sudden we have this little sine wave. You want it to go a little further? No problem. How about we change five to 20? And all of a sudden we have a sine wave going off to the right. What if we want to move it up a little bit? So you could say something like sine of X plus 10, that's going to be your offset. It's going to move it up.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/equation-driven-curve?u=76281980&t=92)** So you can do a lot of cool things with this equation here. Now if you want to switch over to a parametric equation, that's going to be based upon a T value. So again, if you put in a T here and you put a T over here, it's going to give you like a one-on-one relationship, which is going to give you that straight line again. And if you want to then put a value for Y, just like we were doing before, if you want to make it, for instance, squared, then you're going to get a shape that's going to look like that based upon T versus just the X. So you can get a little more complicated equation. In fact, a recent project I've worked on, we had a reflector that we're doing for a light. And this actually came in really handy because they determined what that equation was to create that perfect shape. I literally came in here, I went to the explicit one, I plugged in that equation, and that shape came out. I used that to make a revolve, made a surface, turned that into a reflector. And very quickly, [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) was able to create a very complicated shape based upon an equation that was driven or created from another piece of software. So it's a very cool and useful tool if you happen to have this type of board.

And so if you click on "Hello", you can go over here and you can modify this to "Hi", for instance, Well, notice all of them changed, right? But this one over here did not, right? So it's a copy of that. So you've using the same size font, the same character heights, the same width, all that kind of stuff. It stays the same. So you can make multiple labels all over your part, and you don't have to keep going into that dialogue box and checking them and adding it again, over and over and over again. It'll definitely saves me quite a bit of time when I've used it in the past.

#### [Using the Mirror tools](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-mirror-tools?u=76281980)

One of them is you can do a sketch mirror, and number two is you can do a body or feature mirror, once you've actually already created some type of a solid. So if you're doing all this in a sketch, it's going to be a big sketch, right? It's going to cause a lot of issues. So think about how you're going to be working with these parts. You may want to just make a more simple part in the beginning and then mirror it as a body when you get into it further down the road here, especially if you have like patterning and mirroring, they can make these sketches very complicated and they're very large for the computer to deal with. So you know, like I said,
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-mirror-tools?u=76281980&t=184)** it just depends on what you're doing. But anyways, once you have this whole thing mirrored like that, any change to the original pattern will adjust all the other patterns that have been mirrored from that. It's a very cool tool. Definitely play with it when you get a chance.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-convert-entities-tool?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So the Convert Entity tool is one of the more powerful tools inside of [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md), and it really leverages that associativety and the parametrics of the software. #### [Adding fillets and chamfers to sketches](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/adding-fillets-and-chamfers-to-sketches?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/adding-fillets-and-chamfers-to-sketches?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) provides us with some handy tools to add fillets and chamfers to our sketches. Now, my basic design philosophy is to keep my sketches as simple as possible and add things like fillets and chamfers in the actual 3D solids. And you can see right up here at top of the screen is sketch fillet as well as sketch chamfer. Let's start with the sketch fillet. Pretty straightforward. You're going to literally choose which points and/or lines you'd like to use and then determine what the radius wants to be. So I'm going to go ahead and either choose the corner point or like this line and then that line. So your choice of how you want to do that. So if you change one, it's going to change the other, 'cause they're both created at the exact same time. If you create them all in the same feature, then they'll be linked together. So if you change one, it'll change all of them. All right, so that is the basic fillet. Now, how about we try the chamfer?
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/adding-fillets-and-chamfers-to-sketches?u=76281980&t=94)** So here's the chamfer. It's a little bit more complicated. So you have a couple different options here. One is the angle and distance, and the other one is distance and distance, right? So angle and distance is probably the most common. So I'll say like 0.25 is going to be my distance, and then my angle's going to be 45 degrees. That's pretty common. So I click on a point. It's going to basically just add that for you. So there's that quarter inch going that direction, there's a virtual sharp here, and then here's your 45 degrees. It's adding that for you. You, of course, can go then and change this if you like. So say, let's change it to 0.5, and you can change the angle if you wanted to once it's already created, no big deal. Easily change and modify those chamfers. Now, if you go back to the chamfer tool, I can also do the unequal distance. So if I uncheck this box right here, I can say, "Hey, I'd like to use a chamfer that's like 0.5 this direction and maybe 0.25 in that direction." So direction one and direction two. But you might say, "Hey, what is direction one and which is direction two, or D1 and D2?" Well, click on this corner over here, and you can see what that is. So generally it's going to be the first direction would be the X, and then there would be the Y. But of course, if you've created this, and it doesn't look like what you want, you can always just change it over here to the 25 and the 0.5 and update that. And there you go. So a couple different changes you can make for these type of chamfers. If you don't like it, go ahead and just undo a few times, and we're back to where we were. All right, over here, sketch chamfer again. Angle distance, I think we already covered. Distance, distance we've already covered.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/adding-fillets-and-chamfers-to-sketches?u=76281980&t=187)** If you're doing things like chamfers, I do want to point out is just because you can do a 32.5 degree chamfer in the computer, right, it's a half an inch long or something like that, like you can type in whatever value you want over here to create these angles or these chamfers. But in reality, if you're going to be making this part, as long as it's not like 3D print, if you actually machine it or something like that, more than likely, you want to choose a standard size or standard angle, right? It's going to be a 30 degree, a 60 degree, or 45 degree chamfer. Otherwise, you're going to add a lot of cost to your part. So keep that in mind that even though SolidWorks will allow you to create whatever you want, it's a good idea to try to choose something that's a common size chamfer and/or fillet. Same thing with the fillets. If you're filleting a part, maybe it's a machine part, I can choose any size I'd like, but the standard fillets are going to come in something like half of an inch, quarter inch, a 16th of an inch, maybe 20 thousandths, 30 thousandths, 40 thousandths, something in those standard sizes. And if you don't know what those standard sizes are, go try to buy a chamfer tool or a fillet tool and see what's available and make sure that you're choosing one of those things, especially if you're making a machine part or something that's going to be using common tooling.

### 6. Reference Geometry

#### [Working with planes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/working-with-planes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/working-with-planes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you're creating geometry, one of the fundamental rules is we need to create a sketch on a face or a plane. So we've got our three fundamental planes here and we have all these individual faces of our part here, but what if we want to put in maybe a hole or something like that at a weird angle, right? Well, we don't have a plane on that weird angles. We have to go ahead and create that. So let me show you how to do that. So first, come up here to Features. Come over here to Reference Geometry. And the first thing in that list is Plane. So choose the Plane and it's looking for some references, right? So the way that references work is the more powerful the reference that you select, the fewer of them that you're actually going to need. So something like a plane or a face, you pretty much only need one reference, but if you give something like a point, you're going to need probably three references, right? So you know, a point is the least powerful, a line is the second most powerful. Then you have faces and planes and so on. Let me show you how that actually works. So first things first, first, reference. I'm going to choose something like this surface right here, right? And it's saying, hey, I only need one reference because you've given me an entire surface. I know where it is. And now [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) is assuming that you'd like to make a reference plane that's a certain distance or an offset away from that original plane, right? So here's the original plane and you can see, there's my offset. And of course, we can click on that and move it around or type in this value here to move that where it is. If you want to go the other way, you can do that as well. So quickly change it, and right here, you can also choose and make multiples of that same offset, right? So there's our offset.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/working-with-planes?u=76281980&t=92)** You can make the mid plane, you can do a bunch of things. I mean, there's a bunch of different options that you can do for that, okay? And let's go back into that one more time. Reference geometry plane. Okay, so now, instead of just making an offset from an existing face or plane, what if I use something like a line? I can choose like a line, like this one right here. Well, it's asking for the line, but it also doesn't know like where around that line is. What is the other reference? So it's going to need a secondary reference, right? So you say, hey, I want to go through that line there, but I also want to use like the edge of this part. So notice, it turns too blue, which means it has enough information to make a plane between this line and that line over there. Now, of course, you can change some of these things here as you want to make it perpendicular or coincident or something like that. But really, all you need is those two lines. Click OK, and there's your plane. Okay, pretty straightforward. And notice, that plane is determined based upon this sketch right here. So if I change that original sketch of where this is, which is right here, that will also adjust that plane. So you can come in here and say, hey, instead of this sticking out as far as it does, I can move it in closer. And of course, now that plane is going to be at a different angle than it was before. So you might want to create a sketch or some other things like that to put your planes in the right location and then use those sketches to define where those planes are going to be. All right, the next thing I'm going to do is come back over here to Planes one more time. This time I'm going to choose like a point so I can, hey, how about like that point right there? And if I choose one point, now you have the point that,
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/working-with-planes?u=76281980&t=185)** so this plane can like pivot around in 360 degrees from that point. Well, I need to anchor to something else. How about this other corner over here? And now, I have it going through that point and through that point. But now you think about if there's like a virtual line between the two points. That plane can kind of rotate around those two points. So what it really needs is a third point. And as soon as you click on that third point, now it shows up as blue, meaning it has enough information to go ahead and create that plane. Click on OK, and there's your plane. Okay, so there's obviously multiple different ways to create planes. The best way to create a plane is choosing a powerful selection, right, and the powerful selection is something that is like a face or a plane already. If you only have a line, you're going to have to choose two lines or a line and a point, for instance, or a plane, or you know, if you're choosing, you know, edge, you can choose two edges. But if you're going to add a point, you're going to have to choose at least three points so it knows exactly where that plane is going to be.

#### [Creating a coordinate system](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-a-coordinate-system?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-a-coordinate-system?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] So anytime you're creating a new part in [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md), it has a default origin, as well as a default coordinate system that's been assigned. Now, we've already looked at this a little bit in the past, but I do want to point out why this is really important, especially if you're taking one of the certification exams. So if you're taking a certification exam, like the CSWP or maybe the CSWA, a lot of times, they're going to ask you to build a part and then assign a coordinate system and then measure that part, see how much it weighs, figure out what the moment of inertia, something along those lines of what you're going to be looking to do. So let me show you how to do it. Before we jump into that, though, let's go ahead and look under Evaluate and come up to Mass Properties. So under Mass Properties, notice it's giving us the default coordinate system, which you see right here, and it's giving us the density of the mass, the volume, surface area, the center of the mass, and everything like that, as well as the moments of inertia, the principle axes of inertia. All this other stuff down here is all based upon this default coordinate system, which happens to be kind of like the middle of my part, and that's really not where I want it to be. So let's go ahead and create another one. So if you look at the part here, you can see we've got a pretty good right angle down here. And if I look at the sketch, at the part, you know, like, if you look up here, we want to make sure, like, these two things are perpendicular, for instance. You can drag it around or something like that. So obviously, the part's not fully defined, but you're looking for something that's a good edge that you can choose, like a right angle. Ideally, it would be a great thing to start with. Okay, so if you are looking at this part here, I'm going to come up here and go to Features, come over here to Reference Geometry, come down here to Coordinate System.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-a-coordinate-system?u=76281980&t=93)** And now you can see it's kind of like sitting down here, and that's really a random place. It wouldn't make no sense, right? So if you're going to do like CNC machining on this part, or you're going to use some of the integrated CAM inside of SolidWorks. Something like that, you want to put that at a reasonable, logical place, like a corner of a part. So how about we're going to put it right over here in this corner here? So I'm going to say, "This location is going to be the corner right there." And you can still see that these angles or these line or the directions is going kind of weird places, right? So we don't really want that to happen. We want the X axis to kind of go along this line here. So that looks good. That's straightening things out. The Y axis, I'd like to go that direction right there. And then Z is coming up, right? That's perfect. That's exactly what I'm looking for, is I'm going to create a new coordinate system. It's going to be there. All the axes have been aligned and we're ready to go. Click OK, and there you have it. So now once you have that established, now you can actually use, like, that front face, right, that side face, the top surface of that coordinate system to start creating sketches on and drawing on. You can do rotations around those different axes and things like that. So you have a lot of power and a lot of things you can do with adding these coordinate systems. And you can add as many as you'd like. And once you've added one, come over here to Evaluate, come back over here to the Mass Properties, and now instead of the default coordinate system, notice I can choose Coordinate System 1, and all these values down here change 'cause they're all based upon where that origin is that we've created from that new coordinate system. So notice the center of mass is now relative to that coordinate system here. If I switch back to the default,
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-a-coordinate-system?u=76281980&t=184)** notice these values change, right? But these values, the distance to the center of mass, are irrelevant 'cause they're coming from, like, some weird place in the middle of the part. You know, how are you going to measure that? Doesn't make any sense. So you need to come over here, create a new coordinate system, and then you can measure directly from that, and it's going to be a lot more logical place inside of your design. So hopefully, that helps you out. Especially if you're taking any of the exams, make sure you for sure know how to create your own coordinate system.

>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/adding-fillets-to-a-part?u=76281980&t=185)** It looks really nice. And now let's jump into the variable radius fillet. So I'm going to delete that one for right now and come over here and come back up here to fillet. And let's go over to the variable radius fillet. This time I'm going to choose an edge like this one right here. And starting my variable radius fillet, I'm going to type in like 0.25 and it's ending over here at 0.9. But I can also choose any one of these intermediate dots here and I can control it down to there. So notice I'm bringing a quarter inch fillet all the way to here, and then it's going to expand out bigger. Of course, you could change the value in this radius as well. So if you wanted to make it like 0.375, you could do that as well. And then you can adjust how that fillet looks as it goes along the line or multiple lines. You can choose a whole chain if you'd like. Okay, click Okay, and there's my filet, pretty nice. Okay, let's delete that one as well. And this time, let's go up here to fillet one more time and choose the face fillet option. Now the face fillet option works to kind of as a problem solver fillet. So most of the time you're going to probably try to use the constant size fillet, but if you have multiple faces, you're trying to fillet around, sometimes these face fillet option will save the day. So right over here, items to fillet, I'm going to choose this face right here. And then in my second box, I'm going to choose this face as well as this face right here. Now let's go ahead and make my fillet bigger and bigger and bigger. So what's happening here is it's going to get bigger and bigger. And as soon as it gets past this one point, notice right here, it's going to jump to that next size, which is right here. A regular fillet would stop there. It couldn't go any further.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/adding-fillets-to-a-part?u=76281980&t=277)** But because I've selected multiple faces over here, it's going to jump past that and it's going to allow me to create this much bigger fillet that will work between all those individual faces. So that's kind of saves the day. If you have a hard time creating a fillet, sometimes that face fillet will save the day just by choosing a multiple faces at one time. Let's go ahead and delete it and then come back here for the last style of fillet, which is the full round, fillet. So click on that one there. And we need three faces to make this happen. So I'm going to choose this face first, switch the next box and choose that face there. And then choose the third box and that one there. And it figures out exactly what size circle will go in all three of these between those individual faces to create a nice full round filet, click okay. And there is your beautiful full round fillet. So you can see the fillet command is very powerful. There's a lot of different features and options of how you can fillet things. My recommendation if you're filleting things though is save the fillets for the end. Create your parts, do all the features and the holes and everything else, and then do all the fillets if you can, towards the end of the design process and kind of group them all together. That's definitely a good recommendation for just making your design process a little bit simpler and flow a little bit better.

#### [Applying draft](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/applying-draft?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/applying-draft?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If you're doing any type of a molding process, whether it's injection molding for plastic, die casting, or pretty much any other type of molded process, you're generally going to need to have draft. And what is draft? So draft is just an angle on the outside of your surface. So it allows the mold to open up, and it's like a tapering on the part, so it easily allows the part to come out of the mold. So, right up here is the Draft command. Let's click on that one here. And as far as the angle, I can choose anything I want here, but generally, if you're doing like plastic injection molding, you generally want to shoot for about three degrees or more. Now you can go less than that, but sometimes you'll end up with issues. So definitely try to focus on three degrees or more of draft for that. And depending on the process, maybe you're doing die casting or something like that, and they might want you to have more or less, depending on the feature. It also depends on the depth of the feature. But, anyways, let's go ahead and start applying that, right? So, right over here is going to be kind of our neutral plane. #### [Using the Shell command](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-shell-command?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-shell-command?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The Shell Command is a great and powerful tool inside of [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md). So something like a injection molded part would be a great thing to use a thin wall for, or maybe a thermal form part or something like that. > **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-mirror-command?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Whenever you're working in a CAD software and you can use something like a mirror command or a pattern command, you're really leveraging the power of that software. So it's very powerful. The options down here. Merge solid, knit surfaces, do all those things. So you have one finished part and then go ahead click okay. So now we have the top half of this. And let's mirror it one more time. This time I'm going to mirror it over here. Over this surface here. Bodies to mirror is going to be that whole one right there. Knit the surfaces, click okay, and there's my finished part. Now it looks exactly like our old part. In fact, now we have, instead of one feature, we have three. But it was a lot quicker and easier to make that original sketch now. Because it's only a one quarter of the part. Any modifications you make to this will automatically propagate through. Well let me show you how that works. So right over here, if you go over and go back and edit that sketch, well, check this out.
>
> **[4:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-mirror-command?u=76281980&t=274)** If I put like another hole, let's just put like a rectangle right here, right? Exit out of the sketch. Well, guess what? That automatically propagates through to all those different mirrors, right? And we can keep going with this. If you're feeling fancy, why not? So grab the mirror command again. This time, let's choose a surface like this one right here. Bodies to mirror. Let's mirror that entire thing. Bam! All right? So you can continue mirroring as many times as you want. And then any change to that original sketch, you know, if I make that one back to being small and maybe I add like one more little hole over here, right? When I get it out of there, guess what? That propagates through to everything else. So it really allows you to kind of multiply your effort and multiply your design very quickly by using the mirror command. And just anytime you're working on any kind of design, the very first thing you want to do is really think, hey, is this symmetrical in any way? Can I make one side of this or one half of this? And if you start your design that way, with the right mindset, you really can make a design that's much quicker to build, but also much more robust. So when you go back to edit in the future or somebody else does in the future, you're not having to replicate work, you're just doing one half or one quarter or one eighth, whatever the division is, and making sure that you've really defined that very well. And it also makes for a lot less chances for errors because everything is going to be symmetrical from that point on. Anyways, that's the mirror command in SolidWorks.

So the first thing I want to do is come up here under Features, come over here and find not only the Lofted Cut. Here it is. So click on Lofted Cut and my profiles. I like to choose the profiles from the little dropdown tree here. So here is my sketch two, and down here is sketch number three. And you can see what happens there is I can actually create that loft. And if you can't really see through the block, which obviously we can't, you can switch your display style from shaded with edges maybe to like a wire frame so you can see a little bit better what's going on down here, right? You can use guide curves just like before. You can also use these starting and end constraints. So you could say like something like a normal to profile if you want to kind of pooch it out a little bit. And same thing at the end, you can say normal to profile and you can kind of tuck it in a little bit. And then based upon these arrows, right, you can add some control of how much you want that to affect the shape. And of course you can just drag the arrows or you can just use the up and downs, right? Sometimes it's a little easier
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/making-lofted-cuts?u=76281980&t=92)** just to create the up and down effects. Once you get a shape that you're looking for, this looks pretty good, right? It's a nice gentle curve, it straightens out, overall looks pretty good. Go ahead and just click on OK. And there is my lofted cut. Switch back over here to the shaded with edges and you can see what that looks like. And in fact, I always like to do something like a section view here and section it across. And you can really see how that shape's going to look. So that looks really nice, right? And after you've got this shape, you might even think about doing something like a shelling command or a thicken command, something like that. So you can then, you know, get rid of this extra material here, right? And just create that shape or refine that shape back to where it is. But anyways, the basic loft or the basic loft cut are created pretty much identically. One's just going to be removing material, one's going to be adding material. They basically have the same constraints, they have the ability to use guide curves, and you can loft between two profiles or 200 profiles. It doesn't matter, I mean, you can really have as many lofted profiles as you'd like. You can have as many guide curves as you'd like, and it just gets more and more complicated from there. But my recommendation for sure is, you know, start creating those profiles, start creating those guide curves and try to make some preliminary lofts as you go to make sure you're making sure the loft is working. Even if it's not the final one, you can always delete the loft and then keep working on more profiles and more guide curves. But make sure that it's going to build correctly as you're going 'cause sometimes I've actually had this problem before when I've done it of creating these very complicated lofted features and then trying to go build the loft, and it absolutely fails.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/making-lofted-cuts?u=76281980&t=185)** And then you have to come back to the beginning and have to start again and really think through how that's going to be designed. Make sure that like each point corresponds with the point of another sketch and so on. So definitely like play with it, get to use it a little bit, really start playing with the program a little bit more before you start getting into the really, really complicated lofts, because they definitely can get kind of cumbersome as you get into the detailed ones. Anyways, that is the lofted cut tool. It's a very powerful tool, quite easy to use, and very similar to the regular loft.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/intersect-tool?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The intersect tool is an amazing tool that allows you to take a face or a plane and use that to slice up a solid body. So Right Click anywhere you want, go over here to tabs, come down here to surfaces, and now i've got the surfaces toolbar. So the first one over here is an extruded surface. Pretty straightforward, it's very similar to a regular extrude, but instead of making a solid, it just makes a flat surface. So I'm going to go ahead and choose that line. >
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/intersect-tool?u=76281980&t=95)** and I want to edit that appearance. So the things I want to intersect is this block and this plane, or sorry, that surface, I mean. So I got those two things here. And then it's asking me what I want to do here? And then you can also choose if you want to get rid of one of these things. So if I say, hey, I don't really want that one, I just want the bottom of that shape, that's fine. Or if I don't want both of 'em, either one, or you could just want the top, that's fine too. So you can choose how you want it. I want both, so uncheck both of those. So I want to keep both. I don't want to merge results, 'cause if you merge results, you just end up with a block again, that's definitely what we don't want. And if you want to consume the surface, you can do that, and so that way it's just going to get rid of the surface because we've already used it to cut this thing in half. Click on OK. And now you can see we've got two individual blocks. Well, here they are, block number one and block number two. So if I hide block number one, notice I've got the top half here. So here's the block on the top half.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/intersect-tool?u=76281980&t=189)** And if I show this one and I hide this one, now you can see I've got the bottom. So this is a great way to make something like a mold or something like that. You have two halves of a mold, you want to intersect 'em together and then make an offset or an indention or something like that, a great tool for that. There's a lot of different uses you can think of inside of [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) for creating and slicing things up. I've seen one that's actually kind of a neat thing to do, and it's actually a puzzle. People will take like a piece of wood and they will create these puzzle pieces that are all over it, that they're going to want to cut with like a laser beam or something like that. And then they literally intersect the two, and then it takes whatever this cool piece of artwork or cool shape is, and it just slices it up with all these puzzle pieces. And then they can take each one of those little pieces and then have it laser cut or molded or whatever they're going to do with it. So it's a really cool tool and there's a lot of things that you can do with it. Think about creative and cool ways you can work with this tool.

#### [Direct editing: Move/Copy Bodies](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/direct-editing-move-copy-bodies?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/direct-editing-move-copy-bodies?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For this section of the course, we're going to be working with direct editing. Now, direct editing allows you to take a solid or a part and just directly do something to it. I don't have to go back through the feature manager and find where that feature was originally created. If you want to move a face, you just move the face. You want to add a hole, just add a hole. If you want to delete something, you can just quickly delete it. So it's great if you're working with somebody else's model and they didn't create it correctly, or there's some problems or features and you just want to make some quick and dirty edits to your part without having to know exactly how that part was created. This also works really good if you're importing something like a step file, right? It doesn't have a feature tree, it's just a dumb solid that's been imported. You might want to modify that. So it actually moves that face up, and it continues, and it expands these chamfers on the outside of the part. So really handy, right? So think about if you're going to do like a machining operation and you say, "God, I wish it just had a little extra material on the top here so I could do a skim cut on the top." Or maybe you're doing some type of a molding operation and the part's just a little bit smaller, you're working with shrink and you just want to move things a little bit. You don't want to go back and change the actual original drawing or sketch, right? You just want to add this as a separate feature that you could suppress or unsuppress as needed. It's kind of at the end of the part. Or if you're working with something that's an imported solid, you can then just operate on that directly without having to go back and try to find where it was originally created. Or if you don't have the option of going back and changing the original features, you can just quickly do this at any point in time. So that is the basic for Move Face right there. So right over here, I move the face. Let's dive in a little deeper.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/direct-editing-move-face?u=76281980&t=93)** Go back to Move Face. This time, instead of actually just moving that face, well what if I chose the chamfers as well, right? Choose those and then click OK. And then, oh, we got to go back up again, here we go. So now, it's bringing the chamfers with us, right? So now, it's moving this face and these face here and it's just making these counter boards a little bit deeper, right? So pretty cool there. Let's go back again over here to it. This time, I've got those three faces selected. Instead of translating them, what if we offset them, right? So what if we do an offset here of a small number? So let's say type like .1 for instance, okay? And what's that's going to do is it's going to try to offset that shape to a certain distance. In this case, it maybe it's too much, right? So you might have to go down to like .05, right? And it's going to push that surface up to that amount. Click OK, and now you can offset that. But notice now these chamfers are smaller because they're not just being pushed up, they're actually being offset, right? Now, over here, let's try it one more time. This time, instead of offsetting it, let's try rotating this, right? So instead of keeping that face where it is, let's try rotating it over a little bit. And you can easily get into trouble here because notice right over here, now this surface is below the other surface. Click OK and see what happens. And hey, it actually solved it, right? So it actually cuts in a little bit here, but it did rotate that surface around and it kept over here, this geometry, in pretty good shape. So [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) is pretty powerful when you're dealing with this. And you can really do quite a few things with this Move Face command. > **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/direct-editing-move-face?u=76281980&t=187)** Let's go back over here to the feature. So to make that bigger, we want to offset it, right? We want to make that a little bit bigger. We want to go the other direction and we got to make sure we're doing a direction that makes sense. So let's type in like, let's say like 20,000s or 30,000, something like that. And you can see it's making this a little bit bigger. So this is an example of like, hey, if you got a bolt that's going in there and it's a little bit too tight and you need to make that hole a little bit bigger, let's just make it 20,000s bigger on the radius, click OK, and then now we've modified just that counterbore. The other ones are still where they were, so I'm only modifying that one right now. Of course, you could select those other ones and modify them at the same time, but it really allows you to quickly directly edit any feature in your design. Now, notice we've got these two Direct Editing features right here in the toolbar. So if you click and drag the History bar back, it'll take you right back before that. Then you can drag it forward for one, it's going to do the rotation, drag it forward one more time, and it's going to make that hole a little bit bigger. So if you don't like these features, you can delete them. A lot of things you can do with this tool. It's definitely a time saver and it's definitely helpful for bringing in and working with imported geometry

#### [Slice tool](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/slice-tool?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/slice-tool?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] If you happen to be working with something like an STL file, which is like a 3D printing file, or pretty much any other imported solid, you have the ability inside of [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) to bring that in and then slice it up, and then you can create some things like a loft or really just manipulate and work with the individual slices of that part. #### [Introduction to Sheet Metal tools](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/introduction-to-sheet-metal-tools?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/introduction-to-sheet-metal-tools?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The point of this video is to show you some of the basic sheet metal tools that are available to you inside of [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md). Now in this course, we don't have time to dive into all of the sheet metal tools, but if you're interested, I have a full course on SolidWorks sheet metal. Definitely check it out, and it'll dive into a lot more detail on the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) of the tools. So right now, though, let's go ahead and turn on the sheet metal toolbar. So right over here, click on any one of these tabs, right click, come over here to Tools, and make sure you've got Sheet Metal turned on, and now I've got a tab for Sheet Metal. Alright, so the most basic of all sheet metal tools is the Base Flange tab. Click on that one right there. I already had a sketch selected, and so now I can define what type of material I have over here. So, right now you can see I'm going blind for one inch, or 0.1. Let's type in like something like five inches, right? So you can see it's sticking out a little further. Come down here I can choose to use a Gauge Table or I can just type in the thickness of the sheet mil that I want to use. I'm going to use something like .O63. My bend radius, let's go ahead and use something like an .030 radius. Now, SolidWorks will allow you to use whatever you want for the thickness and whatever you want for the bend radius, it doesn't care. But in real life, most of the time sheet metal comes in standard gauge thicknesses. So, it would definitely be recommended to choose one of those thicknesses and one of those bend radiuses based upon the standard thicknesses of the material that's sold. And keep in mind, each material has different thicknesses. So, like, 16-gauge aluminum is a different thickness than 16-gauge steel, which is a different thickness than 16-gauge stainless steel. Make sure you're verifying what the thickness is for the material you want to use.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/introduction-to-sheet-metal-tools?u=76281980&t=92)** Same thing with the bending radius, right? You can type anything you want in this box here, but in reality, most bending tools come in standard sizes like an .030 radius, an .050 radius, a 16th of an inch, you know, quarter inch, half inch. And then they probably also come in metrics sizes of like one millimeter, two millimeter, three mil, and so on. So make sure that whatever you're choosing for your design is something that's readily available so that you don't have to build some custom tooling or make some weird thin piece of sheet metal. Anyways, a couple other things over here is the Bending Allowance, right? So the most basic one is just the K factor, but the option for using like a bend allowance, bend deduction or something like that, gives you the ability to actually make sure your flange lengths are exact, and generally you're going to be working with a sheet metal supplier who will tell you those values. So you'll say, "Hey, I'm going to use" "a 16th of an inch in steel." "I'm using an .030 radius, it's got a 90-degree bend," "what is the bend deduction that I need?" And they should give you that information and then you'll have the exact length of the flanges. But if you don't know that information, stick with K factor, 'cause K factor pretty much always works. Okay, now I have a basic sheet metal part here. Click Okay, and there it is. Okay, I do want to point out one thing, if I go back to that sheet metal part, notice where the sketch is. So the sketch here is on the outside. I also have the option to move the material to the inside or outside, your choice, so, I can flip it from the outside to the inside, just like that. So make sure whatever you're expecting the material to be on, you're verifying that your sheet metal part is being built that way.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/introduction-to-sheet-metal-tools?u=76281980&t=184)** All right, once you have that, now I can create a couple of other things. So, the second most common thing is going to be an edge flange. Click on an edge flange here, it's pretty basic. I just go ahead and choose an edge or a corner. I can just drag that edge flange down, right? It's going to adopt all of those sheet metal parameters that we used before. So that .030 radius is still being adopted, right? Now you can change to a different radius if you want to, but in general, you want to set all that up in the beginning and then continue to use that radius throughout your part unless you're making some special T bend or some special big radius or something like that. Anyways, we can also determine, you know, the length of the flange. So, let's say it's going to be like one inch right here, and we can determine how we want that one inch applied, right? So, do we want use it from the outside virtual sharp, or do we want the actual length of the inner virtual sharp, or do we want to basically just make to the tendency of the bend? So, a couple different options of how we want this to be applied. And then the same thing over here, you have the option of making the material on the inside, material on the outside, you can make the entire bend on the outside, or you can come to a virtual sharp. So, this one has the most likelihood of working all the time is having the entire bend and the material on the outside. But this first option here will actually keep the flange the exact same length it was before. So, you might want to play with a couple things like that. When you're done or happy, click Okay, and now you've got a sheet metal bend. All right, come back up here one more time. Now I can do multiple bends at the same time, right? So, here's my flange parameter,
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/introduction-to-sheet-metal-tools?u=76281980&t=277)** if I choose an edge like this one over here, it's going to allow me to make a flange, right? So I can use the Blind motion, or I can go up here to like a [Vertex](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Vertex.md), so I can say, "Hey, I want to make this flange" "the same length as this other one," right? So I'm going up to a Vertex. Same thing over here is I can push that material maybe to the outside, so I have this open bend region over here, which makes things a little bit easier because you don't have to be cutting in to the other material, okay? Now notice, I can add that flange there. I can also choose other edges like this one over here. If I choose another edge, I can say, "Hey, I want to add a flange" "on this side and that side," right? I could come over to this side of the part, I can add more flanges as I go around the part and do all this as one shot, right? I even have the ability to go over and do these rounded edges. But sometimes if you do a rounded edge, it's going to give you some heat. It's going to say, "Hey, this is not going to work" "because I have to stretch that material around there." So we probably don't want to do that one right now, but I will show you that you can do it, if you come up here and you go to edge flange, and you can say, "Hey, I want to use this edge right here," right? And I can say, "I want to bring that out." I'm going to go around here. Now this is going to not really be that easy to make in real sheet metal because this material has to be deformed in that region. So this may be a stamped part or something like that. If you want to pull that material around, it's going to have some custom tooling to make that happen. But, you can do it, right? SolidWorks will allow you to do this, and it's pretty powerful what you can do. So if I can click over here, I can create that whole piece as a sheet metal part all in one shot.
>
> **[6:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/introduction-to-sheet-metal-tools?u=76281980&t=370)** And notice over here, these actually come together. So, a very powerful set of tools. You got the base flange, you have the ability to actually up here to convert to sheet metal. So if you already created a part and it's not sheet metal, you can then convert it over to being sheet metal. You can do things like lofted bends, you can do miter bends, hems, all kinds of things over here, and a whole bunch of things as far as forming tools and gussets and tabs and stuff like that. We don't have time to get into all those in this course, but definitely take a look at, play with them, and if you're really interested, take a look at my other course, "All About Sheet Metal." Thank you.
