Well, a structural member is basically any shape that's a standard shape or a custom shape, your choice, that you can bring in and define inside of SolidWorks. So you can use construction geometry, you can just type in values, however you want to locate these holes is fine, and if you want to add more holes, just go back up here to that point command, turn it on, add another hole. If you added a hole and you don't want that hole, click on the point and hit delete to get rid of it. Once you're done, go ahead and click on the green check box right here or over here, and there are all your holes added to your part. You can then hide the sketch, and you can see how quickly and easily we were able to add all those holes to our part. And if you didn't know off the top of your head the exact size of that counter bored hole for a socketed cap screw, you'd have to go look all this data up or figure that out, so it's really going to save us a lot of time. And in fact, if you decide later on you don't want to use that style of fastener, you could go back over here, edit that feature, and switch from a socketed cap screw to any of these other things. I say, hey, I want to use a button head, notice all of them change, click okay, and just that quickly, I'm able to switch from a socketed cap screw to a button head cap screw. Anyways, that is the hole wizard, very powerful tool to be used.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/getting-started-with-the-hole-wizard?u=76281980&t=278)** Pretty much any design has a hole or a tapped hole or anything like that, definitely try out and use the hole wizard.

#### [Hole Wizard Slots](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/hole-wizard-slots?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/hole-wizard-slots?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] For this video, we're going to be learning about the Hole Wizard slot. Now the Hole Wizard slot's been around for a while, but I want to jump in and show you how that works. The first thing you want to do, though, is select a face you want to put those slots on. Come up here. Under Features, come over here to Hole Wizard and fire up the tool. Now, right over here, you can see, under Hole Type, the bottom row here is a Counterbored Slot, a Countersunk Slot, as well as a regular Straight Slot, right, so let's go ahead and choose the Countersunk one for right now. And then I've got a system of ANSI inch, but you could choose any one of these other systems here. And as far as the type of screw right, I can use a 100-degree screw or an 82-degree screw, your choice. I'm going to choose the 82. And then as far as the fastener size, I want to use a 3/8, which looks fine, but any of those would be okay. Now come down here to the dimensioning scheme. So you can dimension either from the center point to the center point, or you can do it based upon the length of the slot. Now a lot of people I've seen on drawings prefer to define the entire length of a slot, especially if it's a Straight Slot. But I actually prefer the point-to-point because when you think about when you're creating a slot, you're trying to create some amount of motion or some kind of ability of motion inside that slot. So say if you want that to slide up and down by one inch, your slot from center to center needs to be one inch long, right? Whereas if you're dimensioning from the end of the slot, that really has to do with the size of the fastener. If I change from a 3/8 to 1/2-inch or something like that, that length of that slot's going to actually change, but the center to center is going to actually stay exactly the same. So as far as designing,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/hole-wizard-slots?u=76281980&t=93)** it makes a lot of sense to go from center to center. Then you can freely change from one fastener to the next, and it doesn't have any effect on the length of the slot. So right over here, I'm going to choose the center-to-center one, but again, it's up to you. Either one is available. The next thing's going to be End Condition. I try to not use Through All. Go over here. I choose, generally, Up To Next for any type of thing, like a counterbore or countersunk screw. You want it to go completely through your part and be ready to have a screw that's going to go completely through that part, right? It doesn't make any sense to have a blind counterbored hole, right? You always want to completely go through a hole. Now, if you have a tapped hole, of course, you'd want to set a depth, then blind depth would makes a lot of sense. You can also go Through All with a counter or with a tapped hole. But for something like this, it always makes sense to go completely through the top part. All right, you can also add a head clearance, far-side countersink, all things like that. But once you're ready, go ahead and click on Positions. And now, anywhere I put a point, it's going to go ahead and add a slot. So let's go ahead and click on a couple of those. And now if you wanted to rotate your slot, you can actually hit Tab on your keyboard. So you can quickly switch between horizontal and vertical just by clicking on Tab. You can place a few, and then when you're done, hit Escape. Now you're still in the Hole Wizard, but you've turned off the Point command, which allows you to add slots. So if you want to add more slots, come up here under Sketch, come over here to the Point command, click on that, and then you can add in more slots, okay? You can still use the Tab command to flip it if you'd like to.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/hole-wizard-slots?u=76281980&t=185)** And then when you're done, hit Escape, and now you're ready. Now at this point in time, you can then position these slots, just like you would regular holes. I do prefer to actually make a sketch ahead of time as a layout sketch to determine where these things are going to go. But you can come over here and create some Smart Dimensions. You can use some, you know, center line, something like that to align and place these slots where you'd like to be. It's all up to you. Again, I prefer to make a layout sketch ahead of time, and especially if you have a whole bunch of slots, and there's some type of symmetry, definitely look into using like a linear pattern, a circular pattern, or something like that so you're only creating one simple slot or feature and then patterning it with some other tools.

### 10. Blocks

#### [Using sketch blocks](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-sketch-blocks?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-sketch-blocks?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Lecturer] Blocks are independent non solving sketches that can be used in a variety of ways inside of [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md). The most common would be to copy a sketch and then use that sketch somewhere else in your design. By creating a block, all the entities in the block become frozen and then can be manipulated as a whole. We can save blocks for later use, as well as open blocks that were created in SOLIDWORKS format or in other formats like the [AutoCAD](../../Skills/Software%20Development/AutoCAD.md) DWG and DXF formats. To use and work with blocks, we need to open up the blocks toolbar, and I can show you how to do that right over here. So right click anywhere in this gray area up here, you can right click and say Toolbars. It's also available actually over here if you right click over here. So depending on where it shows up, notice, actually, I think I actually turned this assembly toolbar on. So you can just turn those off, move 'em wherever you want. You can actually even dock them over here like on the side of the screen. But for right now, let's just go ahead and leave it here floating in my window. So the first thing I want to do here is I have the option to insert a block or I can create a block, but right now that's grayed out. So the first thing, let's go ahead and just insert a block. So I want to start a sketch though. I want to start a sketch here and now I'm in that sketch mode. >
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-sketch-blocks?u=76281980&t=186)** You spend a lot of time making that perfect cutout, then you want to use that throughout your company or throughout your design, you don't want to edit it. But there may be some reason you say, hey, I want to edit this block. You can jump in here, you can say, hey, I want to add something like, let's make another hole. So now I've created a new block, I can then move it around, and now I have a non-solving entity. So you can either use existing blocks or take some geometry you've already created on your screen and turn it into a block. It's also great if you're going to come up with maybe like a whole pattern that you're going to use throughout your design. So here it is coincident,
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/mating-parts-together-in-an-assembly?u=76281980&t=186)** but you have all these other options, right? You have parallel, perpendicular, things like that, and you can click on some of these things, it's just not going to work, right? If you say perpendicular, it's already trying to be coincident and it's already concentric, right? So there's no way you can make a perpendicular mate here. So you have to choose something that's going to make sense. And [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) is smart enough to choose, hey, you probably want this to be coincident, but parallel would also work, but parallel, if you add that, that's kind of a wasted mate, right? Because it's already parallel. So coincidence is the only one that really makes sense here. Click on the green checkbox and away you go. All right, now let's look at this part again. So here's the blue part. And notice it still has that little negative sign, right? That's because this thing can still spin around, right? So if you wanted to keep this thing from spinning around, for instance, inside of there, if you spin or twirl that down, you can see I still have these like fundamental planes when I created that part, right? I have these individual features. So what if you came over here and said, hey, well the, you know, like, like this one right here, the top plane, I can select something like that inside of a part and say, Hey, I want to make that top plane to the top of this. And because they actually can't touch each other, the only thing that really makes sense here is parallel. I want to make this plane inside of here parallel to the top of the part. Sure, click okay. And now if I look at this part over here, notice the little minus sign is gone. It is fully mated, it's fully constrained. You can't move around, right? So that's what you kind of want. Sometimes something like this is okay to be, you know, not fully Mated, but sometimes you definitely want to have everything fully constrained. All right, so the next one's going to be this one over here. Let's go ahead and choose another Mate. This time I'm going to choose this surface here
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/mating-parts-together-in-an-assembly?u=76281980&t=280)** and I'm going to spin my model around and choose the surface right over here. So now those two things come together, right? And if I want to move this around, I can still just drag this around, but it's not really where I want to be. So you might say, well how about if I made something like the bottom surface here, touching the bottom surface down here, right? What happens then? So those just come together. So now this thing can slide forward and backwards, right? That works pretty good. And notice I'm choosing faces, right? I'm trying to choose a face, I'm not choosing edges, right? And if I did, let me show you what would happen if I chose an edge. Let's go ahead and delete a few of these mates. So right over here, let's delete that mate that I created over here. And notice they all show up down here at the bottom, but also they show up in the part itself. So if you click on that part, if you click on the twirl down, the mates that are attached to that part, I can delete them, right, from here or from the folder down here at the bottom. All right, so now this is back to being free floating. I can do whatever I want with it. So instead of choosing a face like I did, I could say, Hey, I want to mate like this face, but instead of choosing the entire face, I'm going to choose just like the edge, right? Well, you think you did a good job, right? But guess what? This part can actually now rotate. That edge is touching, right? There it is, you can see the edge is touching, but the part can still rotate on that edge. So it's not a very powerful mate. It'd be way better if I were to choose this entire surface here with this entire surface there, and then all of a sudden those two things to come together.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/mating-parts-together-in-an-assembly?u=76281980&t=372)** So keep that in mind. So definitely try to choose faces or planes as your primary mating, attachment or selection, you know? Then if you can't choose one of those things, maybe you'll choose a line. And finally, the least powerful and worst choice is a point. Also, if you make a part, you made it together and then you go back and change that part. For instance, if you were to choose like a point, like one of these over here, and then later on you went and added a chamfer or a radius to that part, well guess what? That point's now gone. And guess what? That mate in that assembly now does not know how to solve the mate because it says, Hey, that point's not there anymore. What do I do? So you're going to have a dirty mate, you're going to go fix it. So generally, try to choose faces, planes as best you can, stay away from points and lines. If you have the option. Sometimes you don't, right? So keep that in mind. So anyways, let's keep going. Next thing mate, let's choose that bottom surface over here. Choose this other face right over here that works together, bring that together. And now I can slide this forward and backwards, right? But take a look at this. So notice right over here, these don't actually line up, right? So this is not a perfect part that doesn't fit in here. There's an issue, right? So either somebody designed this thing wrong or it's never designed to fit in here, but it doesn't really matter, if I want to mate these two things together. This is where you might want to use like a line. So I could say, hey, that surface right there, and maybe like this whole line right here, have them come together. So now that's fully defined, it's attached to that line. But you know, if I were to make a change to this part and get rid of that line, like if I radius that corner, all of a sudden that Mate's going to fail.
>
> **[7:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/mating-parts-together-in-an-assembly?u=76281980&t=464)** So it's a fairly lightweight mate. It's not a good way to design things, but sometimes when you're working on a design, you have no other options. Anyways, those are some of the basic tools and techniques for mating things together. It's a basic tool. And again, it might sound strange, but just say those Mates out loud when you're starting to think about it. And it really helps you to organize your thoughts of how you want to connect one part to another.

#### [Downloading internet parts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/downloading-internet-parts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/downloading-internet-parts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the best ways to design and work with [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) is to not design the components at all. In fact, head over to the internet and download those components for free. Generally, there's a ton, thousands of components, thousands of different manufacturers actually, who are offering their components in a 3D CAD format available to anybody who needs them. So right over here, I've got a couple different examples of content central places or content suppliers, who you can download, just sign up and download some of the components. A lot of times when you're working with a design, you may be designing a couple of the key components, but things like the [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md), the brackets, or circuit board components, or things like that, like these are all available online. So just go ahead and download them, it makes your design so much easier. In fact, generally makes your design look a lot better too because the content you're downloading is a lot higher quality than something you might make. I mean, if you're going to bring in a screw, you might make a really simple revolve to represent the screw, but it's not going to be the full detailed screw. Whereas if you go to McMaster-Carr, for instance, you could download the screw and it's got all the threads and everything like that. so you really have a very accurate model of what you have. So first things first here, though, right over here, 3D ContentCentral, this is the Dassault Systèmes or SolidWorks repository for this stuff. And you can see there's just a ton of suppliers down here, and all types of different components, from circuit boards to hydraulics to motors, and so on. And if you look at all suppliers, it's actually a massive list. So look at all these companies who are providing their components for free. So you can just literally drag and drop, save them to your computer, and utilize these components inside of your design.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/downloading-internet-parts?u=76281980&t=94)** So definitely take a look at 3D ContentCentral. The next one that I like is called GrabCAD and its GrabCAD community. You have to be a little bit careful with this site because the people who are uploading these components or these designs, they're just individuals, right? Or maybe they work for a company, but they're not verified, right? So download with caution, assuming that they've done a fairly good job, but it's probably not perfect. You want to definitely double-check their work or double-check the CAD file. But if you're downloading something, like maybe you're just doing like a shop layout, you need a forklift, well, you can download this forklift right here and utilize it in your shop layout. And you don't really care if it's perfect or not, it's just giving you an example of what it might look like. So definitely take a look, there's just so many things you can search for, whatever you want in here, and you can download some very, very elaborate models that have must have taken weeks, months, maybe even years to create. So, definitely a great resource here under the GrabCAD community. The next one is McMaster-Carr. I use McMaster-Carr all the time. They are a great resource. They're basically a huge online hardware store, and they have locations all across the United States. And generally, depending on where you're living or working, they can deliver same day a lot of times. So anything you want as far as like hardware or supplies, it's a great location, and they provide all the CAD files too. So take a look over here. Under Screws and Bolts, you can click over here, you can say, "Hey, let's go ahead and grab a socket head cap screw," for instance. And you can select over here by the size. So I think we're going to look in the inch system. And I'm going to use the 3/8"-24.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/downloading-internet-parts?u=76281980&t=187)** And the length I'm going to do is a 1 inch. Now, you can see, because I've selected all this stuff on the left-hand side, I have a few options where I can choose something like a alloy steel or maybe a stainless steel or a mill spec. You have a whole bunch of different options here for which screw you'd like to bring in. Once you find the one you like, just go click on that. And you can see right over here you have Product Detail or you can download the 3D CAD file. So if you click on Product Detail, it gives you all information about the screw. And you can also choose to download it, not only in just SolidWorks, but any of these other formats as well. So, but right here, SolidWorks, click on Download, it's going to drop it into your file system, and I can just go ahead and double-click and open that up. And right over here, there's that screw, that quickly. I mean, there's no way you're able to create that on your own without that. When you're bringing in screws, though, like this, I do recommend you doing a couple of things before you start working with it. One is you've got all this detail, like these little serrations here and the threads. Like, a lot of times, I'll just go to that and I'll say suppress. Just get rid of that altogether. The threads, they might look nice at first, but you probably don't want them active, so just go ahead and suppress them. So you get kind of like a good representation, not perfect, but this model will load and operate a lot easier in SolidWorks, especially when you have a lot of hardware. You don't want to bring all that overhead in of the helical threads and the neural links, stuff like that. It doesn't make sense. It also makes it a lot easier to meet this together with components in your design without having the helical cut threads and stuff like that in there. So I definitely recommend kind of making a simplified version for the hardware and then go ahead and save it out to your system. Of course, it makes a lot of sense
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/downloading-internet-parts?u=76281980&t=280)** to save it somewhere important, right now it's just in my Downloads folder. I would put it somewhere on your file system or in your library. And this is where I want to point out, right over here is, your library is right here. So Design Library. You've got a whole bunch of things in Toolbox. So we already looked at Toolbox a little bit, but you have your internal Design Library. Inside of there, you have things like annotations and features and forming tools, parts, and right into here, under there parts, you have a folder called hardware. And already you can see there's some components in there. Well, you can keep adding to this. So if you have a bunch of McMaster-Carr hardware, you might want to make a new folder right over here. So, you have a new folder, put it in here, and then save out this component to that. Right over here, all you have to do is click on that plus icon right there and add this component to that library. Give it a good name, though. I definitely recommend giving it a good name, not just like socket head cap screw, but like giving it a name that you can find it in the future. Because if you start filling this folder up with a ton of these screws, you really want to be able to source and figure out what those are really quickly. And having them in your own library does make a huge amount of sense. Once you've added things to your library, of course, they're available to all of your assemblies. You can easily just drag and drop them in. You also have the SolidWorks content here. You also have a bunch of things here. You've got the 3D components from PartSupply, and you also have the Toolbox. So a bunch of things in your library, and those are all configurable. So, you can, again, bring them in, you can import them, you can save things to them, you can delete things out of them. Great way to work inside of SolidWorks by just putting things in there, especially things you're going to be using over and over and over again. Anyways, that is working with downloading and importing components into SolidWorks.

Okay, so that's the linear/linear coupler. How about this next one, which is going to be just a basic distance, right? So you're saying, hey, how far do we want to make this distance apart? So right now we have 1 inch is the current measurement, but now we can set up the limit, right? So I might say something like 0's going to be in the minimum, and for the maximum, let's make it like 10, right? And then click Okay. So now it's starting at 1, but I've limited, I can't go any further than 10, and I can go to 0. 10, 0. 10, 0. Your choice, right? And then you can slide anywhere within that range, no problem. But it's keeping that motion within that range. So that's your limit distance. And then you can do exactly the same thing over here with an angle, right? So if you have something like this surface here, that surface right there, and you know, you can of course flip these things around however you want to do it. But something like that, you can determine what angle you want to start at, and then what is your maximum, and what's your minimum. So say like, 0 might be your minimum. And your maximum may be 180. And you're starting with 30. Click Okay, and now these components here can go all the way open, and all the way closed, right? But it can't go any further than all the way open or all the way closed. So there's your limit of your angles. So you've got limit here. You've got a coupler between the two of these. You've got the profile center.
>
> **[6:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/mating-parts-with-advanced-mates?u=76281980&t=371)** You've got the width mate. All these tools are very powerful, especially if you're doing more complicated mates and you don't have a basic or an easy way to do it using the standard mates, definitely take a look at some of these advanced mates. They can definitely save you a lot of time.

### 13. In-Context Modeling

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Linking sketches to other parts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/linking-sketches-to-other-parts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/linking-sketches-to-other-parts?u=76281980&t=0)** - In-context modeling is one of the most powerful features of [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md). It allows it to tie one part and link it over to another part. So new modifications to the first part automatically affects the second part, and we can create robust structures and assemblies all from one driving part. Let's go ahead and dive in and learn about in-context modeling. Let's talk about in-context editing. Now in this example, I've got a base part right here and I've got a cover. And what I'd like to happen is that the cover will automatically adjust in size based upon the size of the base. Now, to do that, we need to do in-context editing. So we're going to be modifying a part in the context of the assembly and then linking that dimension to the size of the base. To do that, what I need to do first is take a look at this part. So I'm going to click on that part here and click on Open. And you can see here we just have a simple revolve, and if I twirl this down, I can take a look at the sketch. And inside of that sketch, you can see we've got some dimensions here. Now, this three inch dimension right here is the one that is controlling the size of that cover. So we want to replace that dimension with actually a link to that base part. But to do that, we need to actually modify this part in the assembly. So exit back out of this and head back over here to the assembly and let's go ahead and update it. And now we've got the assembly open. So click on the part itself, and that is the part I want to modify, right? So if I come up here, instead of opening the part, I can actually come right over here and say Edit Part and notice what happens to our screen. Notice over here that this icon,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/linking-sketches-to-other-parts?u=76281980&t=95)** or actually the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) here becomes blue, showing that that's what we're actually editing right now. And over here, the icon changes. So if we want to get out of this mode, we can go back to the assembly mode, but we want to stay in this mode, right? We're editing a part inside of an assembly and we know because we've already opened up this part and looked at it that that revolve has a sketch. And inside of that sketch is that three inch dimension, right? So we want to get rid of that three inch dimension, hit Delete, then notice if I grab this outside edge here, I can pull it and make this bigger or smaller, right? That's what we want to control. And we want this line right here. I'm going to go ahead and hit Control on my keyboard and I'm going to choose the outside edge of the base part. I'm going to say these guys are going to be colinear. So notice everything turns to black again, right? It's fully defined. And we got rid of that three inch dimension, and instead we replaced it with a colinear relationship. Okay, now let's go ahead and exit out of that and then exit back into the assembly. Now everything looks identical, right? Nothing changed, right? It's the same exact assembly. The parts look exactly the same. Well, that's because we haven't changed the base yet, right? So if I go back and I want to modify the base, I'm going to open it up and I'm going to go ahead and change the base. So I'm going to say inside this base, there's also a sketch, and in this case, there's that three inch sketch. So I'm going to make that one let's say 10. Why not? Make it a lot bigger. So now there's our big old base.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/linking-sketches-to-other-parts?u=76281980&t=187)** And if I switch back over to my assembly, magically it should update. Let's see what happens. It says models have changed. Should we rebuild the assembly? Yeah, let's do it. And just like that, we are able to make the new cover so it automatically adjusts. So no matter which size the bottom is, the cover will automatically adjust in size so it always fits. Pretty handy, right? That's in-context modeling. And all you need to do is edit a part within an assembly and then link together a dimension or create a relationship or create some type of dimension that is related to that other part.

And so if you change the layout on the top level assembly, all the linked components will automatically adjust. So let me show you what I've got here. There's a lot of different options of things you could do to make this shape, but for this, I'm just going to use the thin feature quarter inch, and then the depth is going to be 10 inches here, and then click okay. All right, so now I've created this one component inside the assembly, it's in context, and if I exit out of that, now you can see here's my part. Now let's go back over here to the assembly, and because they've got everything shown, I can then hide the layout sketch if I'd like to. >
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-hole-wizard-in-context?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Can you imagine if you had a plate like this one you see on the screen here, and you wanted to put some holes that were directly below these bolt holes here or these counter board holes, and the tapped holes would just follow it around. I could move it to this side, I can move to that side, and those holes would automatically follow where it is. Well, guess what? We have some in-context features. We've already seen a few of them being used, but now I'm going to show you how to use the hole wizard in context. It's pretty straightforward. Let's go ahead and choose this feature right here or actually this part. And I'm going to say I want to come up here and edit the part, right? So I want to look at this top surface here, and let's go ahead and click on the top there so we can see the top straight down. And now let's go over here to the hole wizard, right? And the hole that I'm looking for is just a tapped hole. It's an antsy inch. I'm going to go with just a through hole or tapped hole. And the size I need is a 1/2-13. So there's the 1/2-13. And end condition, let's go up to next. And that one looks pretty good. Let's make a thread class of 2B. All right, now go over our positions. Now, because I'm in-context, wherever I happen to snap these holes to, notice I'm just snapping to the middle of those existing holes. That's the placement. I don't need anything else. That's it, I'm just selecting those holes of where they are in-context, placing them, and now click on the green check mark and we've placed the holes. So notice we've got this part at the top here, and now directly below, we added these holes to the assembly. Let's exit out of that in-context feature, right?
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-hole-wizard-in-context?u=76281980&t=94)** So now you can see I've actually got those holes in the part. And if I open up that part, here it is. You can see I've already added these tapped holes to that part, right? But they're 100% in-context, right? So if I move this plate over here, they don't follow along. No, just kidding. They're supposed to, right? Why are you guys not following along? Well, you have to rebuild the model, don't worry. So right up here, hit the red light/green light rebuild, click on that and magically, those holes are going to travel over here directly underneath this plate. So wherever you happen to go, you move it here, you hit the rebuild, you automatically update and rebuild over there. And those are 100% in-context. So wherever I put the red part, it's automatically going to drive where the holes are in the connecting part of the gray part. Just that easy. Then, of course, we can go ahead and add in our bolt, and the bolt will obviously follow that as well. So I can go over here to an assembly. I'm going to create a mate using the bottom of that and the bottom of that. Whoa, uh-oh, something went wrong. Not the bottom of that. We didn't spin it around far enough, right over here, at the bottom of that, spin it this way, over here to the top of that, those come together. And then, of course, the outside boss there, the outside boss there, and those come together. All right, so there's our mate. Of course, we can do that a few more times if you wanted to, or you could grab one of those toolbox components and drag and drop it into here as well. But basically, wherever we happen to move this plate, hit rebuild, those holes automatically go. And that's how you use the in-context hole wizard.

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-design-tables?u=76281980&t=0)** - By using design tables inside of [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md), we have all the power of [Microsoft Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) linked to our SolidWorks files. We can individually tie dimensions to cells inside of [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md) spreadsheet and then drive that part from those cells. It's a pretty powerful thing. #### [Selecting component configurations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/selecting-component-configurations?u=76281980)

So if you go back to the part over here, you could come over here and go under Custom Properties, and say, "Hey, let's add a description into this part." So now, I'm going to go back over here to the drawing. Hold on, that's the sheet format. Go over here to this drawing, here it is. And now, all of a sudden, that description shows up here right here, new part 1. So those are all linked together in those drawings,
>
> **[12:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/working-with-drawing-templates?u=76281980&t=750)** you can easily modify any template. You can start with any template, modify and do whatever you'd like to it, make sure you add your logo and your company information, whatever the right tolerance thing and things like that. Add all that information in, and then when you're ready, you can then use that template for any of your different documents, and definitely recommend you creating templates for each and every different-sized drawing you're going to be using, as well as both metric as well as English units. #### [Choosing the correct projection angle](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/choosing-the-correct-projection-angle?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/choosing-the-correct-projection-angle?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's talk about projection angles. Now, there's two basic types of projections inside of [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md), or actually, in any style of drawing. And that's first angle projection or third angle projection. Now, if you're not familiar with what this means, that means when you have a part right here like this and you make a view of the right-hand side of it, so here's the top view and you look at the right side, which side of the part is that actually going to be on? So right click on any place in the drawing view here. You can come down here and click on Properties. And you can notice there's the type of projection here. It's either the first angle projection or the third angle projection. Now, it depends on where you happen to be in the world. If you're in the United States, third angle projection is generally common. If you're pretty much anywhere else in the world, first angle projection is very common, and generally on your drawing, somewhere it's going to tell you which projection mode you're going to be using, right? So in this drawing, it's not there though, right? So we need to make sure that we are adding a projection mode to your drawing template to make sure you're letting people know how to interpret your drawing. So it's very important to make sure you do that. I believe that's also a drawing standard from ANSI or ISO telling the users of the drawing what type of standard you have. But let's explain this a little further. So the way I learned this was if you took a part like this one right here, and you put it in the bottom of a big mixing bowl, and then you slid that part up the right-hand side of the bowl, which side of the part would you see right over here? And then same thing over here on the other side. Let's go ahead and make another projection. So drawing, projection view. I'm going to bring a view over here and one to the top, and then one down here to the bottom.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/choosing-the-correct-projection-angle?u=76281980&t=95)** Okay, so those are a bunch of different projections of that same part. So again, it's a mixing bowl. You're going to roll it up to the right. You can see this side over here. You're going to move it up to the top. You're going to see the triangle over here to the left. You're just going to see the side and down to the bottom. So that is the your third angle projection. Now, let's actually go and look at the part, right? So here's the part, and you can see here, over here on the right-hand side of the part is this little hexagon. You got a little triangle at the top, and that's pretty much what the part's going to look like. Now, let's head back over to the drawing and switch from third angle projection over to first angle projection. So notice over here, I've got that hexagon over here on the right-hand side. Now let's switch over here under properties to first angle. Click over here, apply changes. And now notice that instead of being on the right-hand side, now it's over here on the left-hand side. Notice the triangle, which was up here, is now down here. So if you didn't know the style of projection you're using, you might build this part completely different than it's actually supposed to be built. So it's very important to know which style of projection you're using. So in this case, a first angle projection would be the same mixing bowl, but you flip it upside down on the table. So you put the part on top, the upside down mixing bowl, and then you slid that part down the sides of the mixing bowl. So in this case here, you would take this part here, you'd slide it down the mixing bowl, and you're going to get this side over here. So that's the difference between first angle projection and third angle projection. Again, it's very important to make sure you know which one you're looking at here so you interpret the drawing correctly.

### 16. Dimensioning

#### [Creating general dimension notations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-general-dimension-notations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-general-dimension-notations?u=76281980&t=0)** - In this chapter, we're going to be learning about drawing dimensions. We'll cover the Baseline Dimensions, Chain Dimensions, Ordinate Dimensions, plus so much more. Let's go ahead and dive in. General dimensions can be added to your part or your drawing view inside of [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) pretty straightforward and easily by clicking on the Annotation toolbar and come over here to Smart Dimension. Now, directly below Smart Dimension here are a whole bunch of other ways we can add dimensions, but Smart Dimension generally picks the best dimension possible. Instead of having to choose individual, like horizontal, vertical dimensions and so on, this Smart Dimension will do most of that for us. So go ahead and choose that one there. Now we have to choose which face of our part is our datum, which one is going to start all of our dimensions. So in this case here, I could choose either this face here or this one over here. Now this one over here makes more sense to me, so I'm going to start there, and I'm going to start dimensioning to certain things. So if I choose like this first hole, I've got a dimension between the two. So click the face you want to start from and then choose the face you want to end to. If you wanted to mention to these holes, before we go any further, I'm going to Escape out of that tool. I'm going to come over here to Center Mark, and let's go ahead and add a couple center marks in here. I prefer to not use the defaults, and get rid of these extension lines. And then just click on a couple of these other holes. Same thing with these arcs right here, I can choose and make a center mark down there. Same thing with these guys here. Let's go ahead and just add some center marks as we go. All right, once you've got the center marks in there, we can continue adding more dimensions. Now, I always want to start from this face, right? Some people will try to go and add a dimension like from here to here.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-general-dimension-notations?u=76281980&t=93)** Can you do that? Yes, but it's not a good practice, right? Because you're now pulling a dimension from a different side of the part. You're not using the main datum target, so you have to have a pretty good reason why you would do this. A better way to do it would be delete that and then dimension directly from this face over here. Okay, so what we call this dimension scheme is a Baseline Dimensions scheme. You keep coming from the same baseline and adding more and more dimensions across your part, and they start stacking up, like you might see right here. And again, jump back over in that tool. Always go back to that same exact baseline. Another way, though, you could dimension things is what we call Chain Dimensioning. So instead of dimensioning like that, I can delete these guys here. I can say, "Well, this one is 1.25," and then from that hole to the next hole, I can add a dimension there. And then from that hole to the next hole, I can add a dimension there. The problem with this style of dimensioning, though, is if there's a tolerance here, so if this is a plus or minus 5,000ths tolerance for this first hole, and then this is also a 5,000th, that hole actually can be 10,000ths, plus or minus 10,000ths from this baseline. And down here, we get another stack of 5,000ths, plus or minus 5,000ths. And so this hole can be plus or minus 15,000ths from this face over here. Whereas if you added a dimension that was from this face here over to this hole here, then that one is constrained to plus or minus 5,000ths from this edge. So that's important to understand the difference between Chain Dimensioning and Baseline Dimensioning. Now another thing we could do is a combination of the two.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-general-dimension-notations?u=76281980&t=190)** So let's go ahead and delete both of these. And in this case here, I'm going to delete these, and I'm going to say, "Hey, these first holes are 0.3 from the edge," and where they start, it's probably not that critical. So instead of a three-place decimal, I can switch over here to maybe like a two-place decimal. Oh, there it is, two places. So I can loosen that up a little bit more, right? So it's a little bit easier to hit that dimension. Then from there, I can say, "Hey, from there, I want to make a dimension to the next hole." And then from there, I might want to make a dimension to the next hole from there. So everything's based upon that first set of holes, and now we're creating Baseline Dimensions from that first set hole, right? And it really depends on your design what's important. In this case here, maybe it's the hole-to-hole dimensions that are more important than from this edge. So in that case here, you could say, "Well, you know, that's really not that important here, so I'd rather prefer to dimension from this hole to that hole." That's my critical dimension stack right there, five inches. And this one here doesn't make any sense. Let's get rid of that one, and that one is more important between this hole and that hole. And that is using the Baseline Dimensioning from this hole here. So again, just look at your design as a whole and figure out where and the best way to add in those dimensions. All right, let's do a few more. Right over here, if I want to make an angle, choose this face here, choose this face over here, and add the dimension that way. Now if we want to add some additional dimensions, we might need a couple extra views. Let's go ahead and add a couple views here. Projected View, I'm going to project right up here to the top, and then maybe one over here to the side. Okay, now when I'm adding more dimensions here, I don't want to choose a different datum surface, right?
>
> **[4:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-general-dimension-notations?u=76281980&t=286)** I want to use that same edge, which is this one here. I want to use that same edge right up here at the top, right? So when I dimension from here to here, I always want to choose that same surface, right? Same thing if you're looking at the view on this other side here, we want to make sure we're choosing the same datum target. So up here, if I'm dimensioning from here to the hole, what I'm using is that front face. So over here, I would want to dimension everything from the same front face here to something else on the drawing. I only have that same hole, so I can't really do anything there. But you'd always want to use that same face in any different views you're using. We have the option of adding a Hole Callout, and right up here is the Hole callout. And right over here, I can choose a hole. And if I click on that hole, it automatically gives me all this information as far as the size of the drill bed and how deep I want to drill and what type of tapped hole and the counter-sink that I'm adding in there, and the degrees. All that information, all with one click. It's pretty handy. So that's how you add your basic dimensions to a drawing inside of SolidWorks. There's a bunch of tools up here, and we're going to be covering a lot of 'em as we go through the course, but that's your basic dimensioning scheme that you might use when you're starting out with your first few drawings.

#### [Creating ordinate dimensions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-ordinate-dimensions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-ordinate-dimensions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's learn about ordinate dimensions. Now ordinate dimensions are very similar to regular dimensions, but it's more of a running style of dimension. So you can keep clicking on items and it'll automatically add a value that goes all the way back to the baseline or the zero point of wherever we create that dimension from. Let me show you how it works. Right over here I've got a part opened up and instead of using the smart dimension, let's go ahead and click on the dropdown and come down here to the horizontal ordinate dimension. So click on that one right there. And you have to choose where you want your baseline of your part to be. So notice right over here, I'm going to choose that as the baseline and notice that shows up here down with a zero. So just go ahead and click where you'd like that to be. Now the tool is active, so anything you click on is going to automatically add a dimension all the way back to this baseline feature. This zero mark right here. So over here I can click on like this hole down here. No problem. How about this hole here, sure. How about this edge here, no problem. And I can just go across the part by clicking on individual items here and they're quickly added to my design. And notice they all reference all the way back to that zero mark. So they all are basically plus or minus 5,000 or whatever your tolerance is 'cause they're all going back to the block tolerance. And it is a real quick and easy way to make a whole bunch of dimensions. Some people don't like these dimensions, but for things like sheet metal or components that have a lot, a lot of components, it would just be an absolute mess if you tried to use any other style of dimensioning. So definitely components that have a lot of features, the ordinate dimension is definitely preferable to use for those type of features because you can just add so many and it's very compact. Of course, once you get done adding dimensions this way or across the part, you can then go and create a vertical ordinate dimension.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-ordinate-dimensions?u=76281980&t=93)** So right over here, vertical ordinate. And I can choose something like this bottom edge right here. And then I can just click on these components right here as I go up. And it can be edges, it can be circles, it can be whatever you want. You can even dimension to the top of like an arc like this. If you hold down a shift, you can choose the top of the arc and you got like that. And then of course if you come back and say hey, well maybe if I add a dimension here, it would kind of look weird over here. Well you can feel free to add another one on the other side, right? So I can say vertical ordinate, come over here, grab a vertical ordinate there, and then dimension some of the things that makes sense on this side. And same thing over here, I can hold down shift and grab the outside of that circle. And if I don't click shift, it'll actually choose the center point of that circle. So a couple different options there how you might want to dimension that. You probably don't want to put both of 'em on there, but it is available, right? So that's your vertical ordinate dimension. And across you can see how many dimensions we're able to add in a very short time period, but also very compact, right? So they're just all very nicely placed here and easy to read and see what's going on. Now my second example here is going to be a circular running dimension, which is similar to an ordinate dimension, but instead of just in a straight line, it's going to go around in a circle. So in this case here, I'm going to go over here to this drop down and come all the way down here to the [Angular](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Angular.md) running dimension. So in that case here we're going to choose whatever's going to be the first point of this thing. So I'm going to choose the outside of that circle and then I'm going to choose this circle here, right? And so notice, I get that zero point right there and you can click and add as we go around that circle, dimensions to all these individual items, right?
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/creating-ordinate-dimensions?u=76281980&t=190)** As we go around all those holes and notice it's a little bit tight. So I can grab that and I can bring that out and notice they'll all come out and you know, they're all basically around that same circle. So that's a quick and easy way you can add dimensions around a circle to points like this and things like that. And it really saves you a lot of time versus having to create like regular angle dimensions. So that's both the running angular dimension as well as the standard horizontal and vertical ordinate dimensions. Definitely take a look at them, especially if you're doing components that have a lot, a lot of features and it's just going to be a mess to do it any other way. The ordinate dimensioning schemes are going to be your friend.

It doesn't make sense right there, though. Okay, how about a Block? We've already looked at Blocks. We can make Blocks or insert Blocks. We've already played those a little bit. But you can take anything on your drawing, and you can just select over a whole bunch of stuff and say, "Hey, I'd like to make a Block out of this, and it's going to bring all that stuff in and allow you to make a Block. Or you can insert a Block, right? So if you already have a Block, you can search for where the Block happens to be, grab it, and insert it into your drawing, just like you could in parts. Okay, how about the Center Mark tool? Center Mark, just grab the tool, select which type of mark you'd like to make, and then click on something like a circle or a hole, and it's going to automatically create a center mark for you. You can use the defaults, or you can, you know, adjust as needed right over here. Okay, next one's going to be a Center Line. Well, this part doesn't really have any reason to have a center line in it, but the center line is chosen very easily by choosing two outside features. So that line there and that line there, of course, it's going to put a line right in the middle between the two. This is clearly not symmetrical. So that center line makes no sense,
>
> **[7:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/adding-annotations?u=76281980&t=465)** but it is between the two lines, and that's how you would create a center line. Let's delete it 'cause it makes no sense. How about a Hatch or Fill area, right? So if you wanted to Hatch, like, say like this little counter board here, you could choose this. You could choose the style and the material or whatever the shape is over here. And then you can select something like an area, right? You can make it solid. You know, if you want to color something in, you can pick something like entire area, like this over here. You click OK, and it's going to fill that entire area in with a Hatch. So a bunch of things you can do with that, whether it's solid or some kind of material or things like that, right? How about a Revision Symbol? If you are adding revisions to your drawings, and you've got some different revisions up here, you can then obviously link or show which of these features apply to that revision, right? And same thing with a Revision Cloud, where you can say, "Hey, this whole thing is in a Revision Cloud, and that's applies to adding that revision." And then the very last thing is basically just a table. So we've got a bunch of tables you can add in here. A General Table, a Hole Table, Bill of Materials, Weld Table, Bend Table, Punch Table, all these are available to add into here. So a Bill of Materials makes a lot of sense for an assembly. A Hole Table, if you had a whole bunch of holes, and it was very difficult for you to define where they all were, you could add a Hole Table in. Or if you just want to reference information in a General Table, that is available as well. So they all pretty much work the same way. You can choose a template. You can choose how many columns or rows you want. Click OK, and that table will just magically show up on your drawing.
>
> **[9:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/adding-annotations?u=76281980&t=557)** Anyways, there are a whole bunch of different annotations, I would say there's a lot more detail we could go into in using all of those, but I just want to show you basically where they were and that they are available so you can dive in and learn a little bit more and work with some of the Annotations inside of SolidWorks.

#### [Using the Design Library](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-design-library?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-design-library?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) design library is a huge time saver and it's a really great way to work inside of SolidWorks. You can think of the design library, just like a regular library. You can have books, you can have information, you can have notes, things like that in your library. And you can check those books in and you can check them back out. Same thing with like a note, you can check a note in and then you can pull that note right back out. Same thing with parts assemblies and so many more things. So right over here you can see on the right hand side, if I [Zoom](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Zoom.md) in, here's my part wherever it is. I have this note right over here, right? And I have a library over here. So here's my design library. If I open that up, I could actually click on this little pin to just lock it out here. So it's sitting out here. Now, inside the design library, I have annotations, I have assemblies, I have features, forming, I have all this information, parts, and these are all preloaded, right? So inside here there's like [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md) or inserts or sheet metal features, knobs, things like that. So I already have this kind of information that's been preloaded into this default library, right? Or design library. But I can add my own library. In fact, right over here I can say, "Hey, I want to add something to this library." Or I can say add a file location. So if I already have an existing design library, maybe on like a company shared folder or something like that that's already being used, I can then link that library over here. It's going to show up as an additional library. I can also create new folders, right? So if I'm using, for instance, like a lot of big master car parts or something like that, add a folder, start making a folder for nuts, bolts, washers, whatever else you're going to want to put in there. And then saving out that information. Now, anytime you want to add something to the library, of course you need to figure out where you'd like to put it.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-design-library?u=76281980&t=92)** So, hey, if I figured out this note over here, there's a lot of information here. It's calling out the material, it's calling it a STM standard. You probably don't remember all this off the top of your head. The black anodized is fine, but you might not remember the mill standard for that. So it makes a lot of sense to save that information to a library. So to do that, just click on it, right? And then click on the little plus, add to library and it says, here's the note I want to add. And I can add something over here, like, you know, whatever you want over here, but al and ano, right? Something like that. And I don't want to put it in parts, I want to put it under annotations for this design library. And if you had multiple libraries that all show up over here and then click on, okay, right? So now I've added that al ano right here to the library. So next time you want to use it, I can just drag and drop, right? Put it wherever I want, add it to a drawing. So it makes it super quick to add any kind of notes right to your drawings. Same thing with like surface finishes or welds, information. And then if you're working in assemblies, you can actually save an entire assembly in the design library. You can have like a mold base, you can just bring an entire assembly directly into SolidWorks and just start from there. And then if you've designed or built other parts that might go in there, save them back to that design library. Again, lots and lots of really cool things you can do with the design library. And then below that, notice the toolbox is there as well. And the toolbox is basically just another style of design library. It's got a whole bunch of fasteners in it. And then you also have content up here, is the SolidWorks content. You can, you know, drag and drop parts in from other manufacturers. So a lot of things you can do with a design library, definitely use it.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-the-design-library?u=76281980&t=186)** And I would definitely recommend if you have a company shared folder or a vault or something like that, definitely create a library for your company on that location. And then share it with all the different users, so you can all be sharing that same library, or you can have several different versions of the library depending on what type of parts people might be working on. But if people do all the work to create like those notes, it's great to be able to share that data so you don't have to keep replicating that same work.

#### [Using Equations for Custom Properties](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-equations-for-custom-properties?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-equations-for-custom-properties?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you're working with custom properties for a part, you have the ability to use equations in the actual descriptions. Let me show you how it works. Right over here you can see I've got a part, and if I come up here to the custom properties, notice I've got the basic description and things like that. But if you look at this box right here called text, it's the type, and instead of just regular text, you can actually choose something like the date and put it in there or the number or yes or no questions. Built into the description. You have the access to then grab that information and use it in your description. So it's a really nice little feature they've added in.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/using-equations-for-custom-properties?u=76281980&t=93)** So definitely take a look at some of the different options as far as bringing in things like a text or an equation, or yes or no questions, number, date. There's a whole bunch of things you can do with custom properties. That's really a pretty neat thing to do, especially because these are all tied to that part. And if you have them automatically updating for you, it's going to save you a lot of time.

>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/adding-assemblies-to-drawings?u=76281980&t=185)** we can add balloons, we can add all kinds of wonderful things to define this drawing a little better. Now, drawings for assemblies are different from drawings for parts. On a part level, we want to define exactly all the dimensions and the material and everything else. Whereas in assembly, we're showing how to put this thing together. And if there's any kind of fasteners, like screws and bolts and stuff like that, they're going to put it together, if there's glue or special procedures. So you don't really want to add a lot of dimensions, but you could add some dimensions that kind of just show like the overall. So I could say like from the bottom of this thing to the very top of this food processor, how tall is it, right? Or how wide is it? So you could add those type of dimensions here just for heads up information or if a dimension needs to be added when you assemble something together. That'll also be another dimension you might add here. But otherwise, we probably don't want to have a ton of dimensions on the assembly drawings. So anyways, that is how you bring in an assembly into a drawing and get a couple dimensions on there, explode out the views, and get ready to start adding in some build materials as well as some balloons to define which parts are in your assembly.

But anyways, once you're done with that first step, that's going to be your first move. Then you just click on done, right? Now, you're still in the explode mode, right? But you've only done that first move, right? And here it is. So if you want to continue exploding things, you can just grab your next item, drag it up, right? Get it to a place that makes sense, right? And then if that's all you want to bring along, say done, right? Grab the next component. Bring that one up, right? Come over here, probably put that just slightly above here, so you can see it's going to go into that slot. Looks good. Click on done, right? How about this little bowl part here that's going to come up here? Going to go maybe right below there. Looks good. Done, right? Come over here to this component here. We're going to bring that one up. That's going to go on top. So you want to think about how you're putting these things together in the right order. So notice what I did right here. So if I click on done, it's being hidden a little bit. You see, you go, ah, it doesn't really look that great, right? It's kind of stuck here. Well, you can go back to that first explode step, right?
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/making-an-exploded-view?u=76281980&t=187)** So here, explode step number one. And you can just move that up a little more, right? To clear it out of the way. You can go to explode step two. That's about the right spot. Have about explode three? We can also move that one up a little bit. So you can refine this as you go, right? You can keep going through this explode process. Any you'll move things around, change the angle of how you want to look at it, things like that is important. And then figure out how you want that to look on your drawing. So like if this is the perfect angle, you want to show up on your drawing, something like that, you can go up here, and you can actually save the display style. So I mean, obviously, use this one over here, but right over here, you can actually save a new view. So click on new view right here. And I'm going to call this one like v1, okay? Click on Okay. And now, I've saved that view. So v1 happens to be my new view. It'll show up right in here, so here's side view. Here's all your standard views, right? But now, it's v1. So anytime you want to get back to that v1 view, you come in here, you go down to v1, and bam, you spin it around, you want to go back to v1, no problem. Come over here to v1, you're back to where you were. Okay? Now, I can bring that into the drawing. Now, of course, you can expand this out a little more. You can add some lines, things like that. But you basically understand the concept of creating the exploded view. And then now, you have Exploded View 2 and Exploded View 1. Your choice, right? And then, of course, right down here are those steps. And if you want to modify it or keep going, you can jump right back in here and continue where we left off, right? I can grab a component, pull it up, I can rotate it if I wanted to, something like that. I can click on different things, however you want to do it. And if you click on it again, you can choose to do another motion, right?
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/making-an-exploded-view?u=76281980&t=280)** Like I can do something like that. When you're done, you click on done, right? So a couple different things you can do with each of those moves. And then if you don't like that explode step, right over here, you can say, hey, you know, I didn't like that one. I can delete it, right? Then hit delete. Take it out, and that's going to remove that operation from the explode, okay? Click okay. And now, we're back to where we were. Now, let's go to that drawing. Back over here to the drawing, and I want to add in that exploded view, right? So go over here to model view. The model is already selected over here, and it's asking you which view to want to bring in. So notice over here it's already check marked over here. I can turn that off, actually. And I can do the manual explode, your choice. And it's asking you which one of the views you might want to bring in. Well, check it out. Right over here is that v1. And if I turn on the preview, there is that v1 view, right? So there it is, but it's not exploded, right? So there is the explode. I want to explode it or blow it up, right? It has a couple different options, right? So here's that first one, and then here's the second one, right? So you have the option of which version of the explode you'd like to do. And notice it's using that v1 view to bring in it. So it's a really good idea to kind of create like the perfect look, save that view, and then that'll be an option when you bring that into your drawing. Hopefully, that gives you a good understanding of how you can take a model, explode it out, and then bring it in, especially with the right view and looking good for your drawings.

> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/adding-in-custom-properties-and-revision?u=76281980&t=92)** you want to make sure whatever revision you have here also matches the revision down here in your drawing. You want to be following some ANSI standards or some ISO standards as far as what should be on your drawing, the sizes, the fonts, and all that kind of information. So definitely look into those type of documents to set up your document and your template correctly. If you do need to make any changes to this information down here, remember, those are all linked together to the assembly. So if I click over here and I say edit the assembly, here's my assembly, those are all stored here in the custom properties. So if you wanted to change from rev A to rev B, you would just change it right here and then go back to that drawing and you should show up with a little B at the bottom of the screen here. So here's my revision B now that matches your revision notes right up here. And now, you've got all that information filled out correctly. Of course, if you're using some type of a PDM system or something like that, a lot of times, the revision control will be handled by that. Also, the information as far as drawn by check bytes, stuff like that can automatically be filled out
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/adding-in-custom-properties-and-revision?u=76281980&t=185)** in the PDM systems as well. So keep in mind how that all works and be aware of those changes that can happen if you're using some type of a vault or PDM system. And then finally, when you're getting done with a drawing, everything looks good, you're probably going to want to save it, not only is a [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) drawing, but also as like a PDF, and you have that option right over here. Just go to file, save as and instead of a SolidWorks drawing, you can just come down here. You can actually save it as any of these different file types here. But right over here is the PDF. And so in that folder, you could just say, hey, 18.5 pdf, save, and now I've got a nice PDF drawing that I can send easily to somebody else. You also have the options of using something like an e-drawings, you can save as an e-drawings that anybody can open with a free viewer. Makes it a lot easier, especially for people who don't have SolidWorks can review and look at your drawings.

#### [Detailing Mode](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/detailing-mode?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/detailing-mode?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Let's talk about Detailing Mode. Now, detailing mode is just a mode to open up an existing document. So the most basic way to open any document is right here, on the screen. You can see it's resolved, right? That's going to go out and [SOLIDWORKS](../../Skills/Software%20Development/SOLIDWORKS.md) is going to open the drawing and then look for each and every one of those components and find them and then open them and then bring them into the document. So it's a reference in them with a hard reference. Now, the next option over here is lightweight. So what that's going to do is going to do the exact same thing. It's going to go find all those documents, but instead of fully loading them back into SolidWorks, it's actually just going to bring in a lightweight version of them. That's going to make things a lot easier to kind of view in a quick manner. However, if you do need to actually do something to that document or to that part, you're going to have to resolve it and then you can actually open it and work with it, okay? The next mode down is detailing mode, right? It's almost like a PDF. It's just going to send this document. It's not going to include any of the references. It basically just says, "Hey, turn off looking for references. "Just show what this document looks like as is." And in any other case, that's not going to happen, right? So if you happen to have like in a resolve mode and you don't have all those documents in the same folder or wherever they were located, it's going to throw a temper tantrum. I mean, SolidWorks is going to go, "I can't find any of these documents, "it's not going to work, it's going to be a problem," and it's just going to have a big scene, right? Same thing with lightweight. It needs to know where those files are. Well, if you put it on detailing mode, it's just going to say, "Hey, that's fine." I don't know where things are, but that's okay. I'm just going to work without them. And so that's why that's been turned on, because before, like, if you're going to send this document to some other person to review or look at,
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/detailing-mode?u=76281980&t=94)** you would actually have to do like, a pack and go, grab all those files, all the related documents, sub assemblies, whatever it is that's in there, and send those all together. So then when you open the document, you would have then find all the references and everything would be fine. They're going to make a couple changes and send it back to you. Well, if you just want to send your drawing over to somebody to have a quick review, you can use that detailing mode. So you can just say only the drawing document. They can open it up in detailing mode and you're good to go. Also, this saves the day if somebody doesn't know what they're doing with SolidWorks. Somebody says, "Hey, here's my drawing, look at it." And they send it to you and they only include the drawing. They have no idea that you're actually supposed to include all the references and all the parts. If you just need to review it, you can just switch over to this detailing mode and then open that up, right? And also, I just want to point out while we're here, under lightweight and also under resolve, you also have the option to load the hidden components. You can use a speed pack or you can use large assembly settings. And of course you can also click on references, right? So it's going to be looking for where are these files at, right? Here is the assembly, happens to be in this folder. So make sure you're linking that together. And if that's not the correct name or the correct assembly you're trying to open, or this folder's not correct, definitely look into there and say, "Hey, where's this supposed to actually be linked to?" And you can actually re-link or change the link right in here as well, okay? But once you have that, let's switch over to the detailing mode. Try opening up this document here. Okay, now right at the top it says, "Hey, this document's open in detailing mode." There's a whole bunch of limitations, and if you can click up here, you can actually see what these limitations are, right? So you can do some things, you can't do other things. Make sure you read through all this information here, but notice how quickly it opened, right?
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/solidworks-2024-essential-training/detailing-mode?u=76281980&t=187)** 'Cause it's not loading any type of references. Now you can do some things, you can add like, dimensions, you can add some notes and things like that. And then when you save it out, that'll get saved with just this document. And then of course, when you open it in resolve later on, guess what? It's going to go and try to relink all those things together and bring those changes into the full document. So it's a fairly useful tool, especially if you have a really large assembly, you have multiple people reviewing or looking at your documents and you don't really have a vault system or something like that, that you can check it in and check it out. This is a great and quick way to just make a review document that you can look at. Again, it's another option to using like, a PDF or an edrawing. I think I'd prefer though, to maybe use an edrawing or a PDF for quick review, because that way you can just pretty much send it to anybody. They don't even have to have SolidWorks. Anyways, check out detail mode for SolidWorks.
